The Five Methods of Escape from Mu'arada
Listing and explaining the five methods of escape from mu'arada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the five Hanafi methods for resolving a genuine mu'arada, and apply them to a hypothetical scenario.

Core Idea
When genuine contradiction occurs, resolve it by proof, ruling, condition, or time (explicit or implicit).

Once a true mu'arada is confirmed, the Hanafi usul provides five well-defined methods to escape the impasse without rejecting any proof. These methods examine the strength of the proof itself, the nature of the ruling, the conditions, or the timing. This lesson systematically covers each method with reference to the text's final passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five methods
The text lists five: from the proof (hujja), from the ruling (hukm), from the condition (hal), from the time explicit (zaman sarih), and from the time by indication (zaman dilala).
These are tools of tarjih (preference).
2
Explain each method briefly
Method 1: Prefer the stronger proof (e.g., more authentic, more direct). Method 2: Prefer the ruling that is more in line with principles (e.g., prohibition over permission in some cases). Method 3: Consider the circumstances or conditions of the ruling. Method 4: Use explicit chronological sequence (later abrogates earlier). Method 5: Use implicit temporal indications (e.g., context suggests which came first).
The text mentions the example from the donkey case: 'Feed your family from your fat donkeys...' is used as an implicit temporal indication (later hadith leads to resolution).
3
Apply the methods to the donkey case
The donkey case was resolved by method 4 (explicit time) and method 5 (implicit time) - the later hadith is preferred and provides context that explains the earlier prohibition.
Also method 1: the prohibition hadith is stronger, but it is not a genuine mu'arada.
4
Practice with a simple scenario
Create a scenario with two equally strong hadiths and determine which method resolves it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو تقديراً، ثم تحول رأيه، فصلى في ثوب آخر على تحري أن هذا طاهر، وأن الأول نجس لم يجز ما صلى في الثاني إلا أن يتيقن بطهارته؛ لأن التحري الأول أوجب الحكم بطهارة الأول، ونجاسة الثاني، وهذا وصف لا يقبل الانتقال من عين إلى عين فبطل العمل به. ومثال القسم الثاني من القسم الرابع: سؤر الحمار والبغل؛ لأن الدلائل لما تعارضت، ولم يصلح القياس شاهداً؛ لأنه لا يصلح لنصب باب المعارضة: قوله: لما تعارضت الدلائل: قال الشارح: فإن عبد الله بن أبي أوفى روى أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حرم لحوم الحمر الأهلية يوم خيبر. وروى غالب بن أبجر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أباح لحوم الحمر الأهلية. كان يكره التوضؤ بسؤر الحمار والبغل وابن عمر وابن عباس يقول: سؤره طاهر لا بأس به. قلت: حديث عبد الله بن أبي أوفى رواه البخاري في صحيحه، والإمام أحمد في مسنده. وحديث غالب بن أبجر رواه أبو داود. وأثر ابن عمر رواه ابن أبي شيبة. وأثر ابن عباس لم أقف عليه. ولقائل أن يقول: لا تعارض في هذا فقد قلتم: إن المعارضة: تقابل الحجتين على السواء، لا مزية لإحداهما على الأخرى في حكمين... إلى آخره، بشرط اتحاد الوقت والمحل... إلى آخره. وليس شيء من هذا موجوداً فيما نحن فيه، فحديث التحريم صحيح، وحديث الإباحة مضطرب، فلم يوجد ركن المعارضة. وحديث ابن أبجر متأخر، ففي لفظه: قلت يا رسول الله! أصابتنا السنة، يكن في مالي ما أطعم أهلي إلا سمان حُمُر، وإنك حرمت لحوم الحمر الأهلية؟ الحكم ابتداء: وجب تقرير الأصول. فقيل: إن الماء عُرف طاهراً، فلا يصير نجساً بالتعارض: فقلنا إن سؤر الحمار طاهر، وهو منصوص عليه في غير موضع. وكذلك عَرَقُه، ولبن الأتان، ولم يزل الحدث به عند التعارض، ووجب ضم التيمم إليه، فسمي مشكلاً؛ لما قلنا، لا أنه يُعنى به الجهل. وكذلك الجواب في الخنثى المشكل، وكذلك جوابهم في المفقود. ومثال ما قلنا من الفرق بين ما يحتمل المعارضة وبين ما لا يحتملها أيضاً: الطلاق والعتاق في محل مُبْهَم يوجب الاختيار؛ لأن وراء الإبهام محل يحتمل التصرف، فصلح الملك فيه دليلاً لولاية الاختيار، فإذا طلق عيناً، ثم نسي: لم يجز الخيار بالجهل. كيفية المَخلَص عن المعارضة: وإذا عرفت ركن المعارضة وشرطها وجب أن تبني عليه كيفية المَخلَص عن المعارضة على سبيل العدم من الأصل، وذلك خمسة أوجه: من قبل الحجة، ومن قبل الحكم، ومن قبل الحال، ومن قبل الزمان صريحاً، ومن قبل الزمان دلالةً. فقال: أطعم أهلك من سمين حُمُرك، فإنما حرَّمتُها من أجل جوالي القرية». فلم يوجد شرط المعارضة. تنزلنا، أليس فيه علة التحريم وهو كونها جلالة؟ ثم يقال: ما الموجب لترجيح التحريم في الأكل، وعدمه في السؤر؟ والله أعلم.
Or by estimation, then his opinion changed, so he prayed in another garment with the determination that this one is pure and the first is impure. His prayer in the second is not valid unless he is certain of its purity, because the first determination obligated the ruling of purity for the first and impurity for the second, and this is a description that does not transfer from one object to another, so acting upon it is invalid. An example of the second type of the fourth category: the leftover urine of the donkey and mule; because when the evidences contradicted, and analogy was not suitable as a witness, since it is not suitable for establishing ... The chapter of contradiction: His saying: when the evidences contradicted: The commentator said: Indeed, Abdullah ibn Abi Awfa narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited the meat of domestic donkeys on the day of Khaybar. And Ghalib ibn Abjar narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted the meat of domestic donkeys. And Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas used to dislike performing wudu with the leftover of the donkey and mule, and Ibn Abbas said: his leftover is pure, no problem. I said: The hadith of Abdullah ibn Abi Awfa was narrated by Bukhari in his Sahih, and Imam Ahmad in his Musnad. The hadith of Ghalib ibn Abjar was narrated by Abu Dawud. The effect of Ibn Umar was narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah. The effect of Ibn Abbas I could not find. And someone may say: There is no contradiction in this, because you have said: Contradiction is the confrontation of two proofs equally, with no merit of one over the other in two rulings... etc., with the condition of unity of time and place... etc. None of this exists in what we are discussing. The hadith of prohibition is authentic, and the hadith of permission is mudtarib, so the pillar of contradiction does not exist. And the hadith of Ibn Abjar is later, and in its wording: I said, O Messenger of Allah! The year of drought has affected us, I have nothing in my wealth to feed my family except fat donkeys, and you have prohibited the meat of domestic donkeys? The ruling initially: It was necessary to establish the principles. It was said: Water is known to be pure, so it does not become impure by contradiction. We said: The leftover of the donkey is pure, and it is explicitly stated in more than one place. Similarly his sweat, and the milk of the female donkey, and the ritual impurity is not removed by it at the time of contradiction, and it is obligatory to combine tayammum with it, so it is called mushkil (problematic) because of what we said, not that it is meant as ignorance. Similarly the answer regarding the ambiguous hermaphrodite, and similarly their answer regarding the missing person. And an example of what we said regarding the difference between what may bear contradiction and what may not also: divorce and manumission in an ambiguous place that necessitates choice, because behind the ambiguity there is a place that may bear disposition, so ownership is valid as evidence for the authority of choice. If he divorced a specific person, then forgot, choice is not permissible due to ignorance. The method of escape from contradiction: And if you have known the pillar of contradiction and its condition, it is necessary to build upon it the method of escape from contradiction in the manner of non-existence from the origin, and there are five ways: according to the proof, according to the ruling, according to the condition, according to the time explicitly, and according to the time by indication. So he said: Feed your family from your fat donkeys, for I only prohibited them because of the village roaming animals. Then the condition of contradiction did not exist. If we concede, is there not in it the cause of prohibition, which is that they are galla (animals that feed on impurities)? Then it is said: What necessitates preferring prohibition in eating, and its absence in the leftover? And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage from a commentary on al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the concept of contradiction (mu'arada) and how to resolve it within the Hanafi framework. The author begins by illustrating a case where a person's initial determination of purity/impurity is contradicted by later evidence, leading to invalidation of the second prayer unless certainty is achieved. This sets the stage for the main example: the leftover (sür) of the donkey and mule. Here, contradictory hadiths exist — one prohibition of domestic donkey meat (narrated by Ibn Abi Awfa, authentic) and one permission (narrated by Ghalib ibn Abjar, weak and later in time). Additionally, companion reports (athar) from Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas show differing practices. The author (likely the commentator) notes that there is no true contradiction because one hadith is authentic and the other is mudtarib (inconsistent), and also the later hadith includes a context that removes the apparent contradiction (prohibition due to them being galla — animals that feed on impurities). The Hanafi ruling is that the leftover of the donkey is pure (tahir), based on the principle that water is originally pure and that the permissible hadith (despite its weakness) is supported by the absence of clear evidence for impurity. The passage then extends this reasoning to the sweat and milk of the donkey, and draws an analogy to the cases of an ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) and a missing person (mafqud), where certain rulings are suspended until clarity. Finally, the author outlines the five methods of resolving contradiction when it does exist: (1) considering the strength of the proof (hujja), (2) the ruling (hukm), (3) the condition (hal), (4) explicit temporal priority (zaman sarihan), and (5) implicit temporal indication (zaman dilalatan). These tools allow the mujtahid to determine which evidence to prefer without rejecting either entirely. The passage concludes with a rhetorical question: why is prohibition preferred for eating but not for the leftover? underscoring the nuanced application of usul principles.

Key Terms
المخلَص (al-mukhlass): The method of escape from contradiction by resolving which evidence to prefer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five methods of escape from mu'arada mentioned in the passage?
A) From the proof
B) From the ruling
C) From the majority of scholars
D) From the time explicitly

Key Takeaway
The five methods (proof, ruling, condition, explicit time, implicit time) provide systematic tools for resolving genuine mu'arada without rejecting authentic evidence.

The Rule of Preferring the Affirming Report (al-Muthbit Awla)
Stating the usul rule on conflicting reports and applying it to three hadith examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of preferring the affirming report, identify its exceptions, and apply it to cases of conflicting reports.

Core Idea
The affirming report is preferred over the negating, unless there is consensus on the original state.

This passage from al-Pazdawi's Usul presents a foundational rule for weighing contradictory reports (riwayat muta'arida): when one report affirms a change (muthbit) and another negates it (nafi), the affirming report is given precedence as a general principle. However, the early Hanafi masters applied this rule with nuance, as seen in the cases of Barirah, Maymunah, and Zaynab. In the Maymunah case, they preferred the negating report due to consensus on the validity of the marriage, showing that the rule is not absolute but subject to istihsan and contextual reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting reports
In each case (Barirah, Maymunah, Zaynab), identify which report is affirming (muthbit) and which is negating (nafi). The affirming report asserts a change from the original state; the negating report preserves it.
2
State the general principle
Recall al-Karkhi's rule: al-muthbit awla. Understand that this is the default preference in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the Maymunah exception
Explain why the Hanafis preferred the negating report in the Maymunah case: all reports agreed on the validity of the marriage, so the original state of non-ihram was considered the consensus. The affirming report (ihram) would contradict that consensus.
This shows the rule is subject to istihsan and ijmali agreement.
4
Explain the opposing view
'Isa ibn Aban held that the reports simply conflict and no preference can be given without further evidence.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a new set of conflicting reports (e.g., whether the Prophet wiped over his khuffayn in wudu), determine which is muthbit and which is nafi, and apply the rule, considering if any consensus exists on the original state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال الكرخي رحمه الله : المثبت أولى.
وقال عيسى بن أبان رحمه الله : يتعارضان.
وقد اختلف عمل أصحابنا المتقدمين في هذا الباب، فقد روي أن بريرة أعتقت وزوجها حر، وهذا مثبت، وروي أنها أعتقت وزوجها عبد، وهذا ناف مبقي على الأمر الأول، وأصحابنا أخذوا بالمثبت.
وروي أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام تزوج ميمونة وهو حلال، وروي أنه تزوجها وهو محرم، واتفقت الروايات أن النكاح لم يكن في الحل الأصلي، وإنما اختلفت في الحل المعترض على الإحرام، فجعل أصحابنا العمل بالنافي أولى من العمل بالمثبت.
وروي أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام رد ابنته زينب رضي الله عنها على زوجها بنكاح جديد، وروي أنه ردها بالنكاح الأول، وأصحابنا أخذوا بالمثبت.
Al-Karkhi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The affirming [report] is preferable. And 'Isa ibn Aban, may Allah have mercy on him, said: They conflict [with each other]. And the practice of our early companions has differed in this chapter. It was narrated that Barirah was freed while her husband was free—this is affirming; and it was narrated that she was freed while her husband was a slave—this is negating, remaining upon the original state. Our companions took the affirming. And it was narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, married Maymunah while he was in a state of halal (non-muhrim); and it was narrated that he married her while he was muhrim. The narrations all agreed that the marriage was not in the original state of ihram; rather, they differed regarding the state of ihram that intervened. So our companions made acting upon the negating report more preferable than acting upon the affirming one. And it was narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, returned his daughter Zaynab, may Allah be pleased with her, to her husband with a new marriage contract; and it was narrated that he returned her with the original marriage contract. Our companions took the affirming.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental usul rule regarding contradictory reports (riwayat mukhtalifa). Imam al-Karkhi states the general principle: when one report affirms a fact (muthbit) and another negates it (nafi), the affirming report is given precedence. However, Imam 'Isa ibn Aban holds that the two reports simply conflict and no automatic preference exists. The early Hanafi masters (mutaqaddimun) applied this rule consistently but with contextual sensitivity. In the case of Barirah, two reports exist: one stating her husband was free (affirming the change from her enslavement), the other stating he was a slave (negating any change). They preferred the affirming report and held that her husband was free. In the case of Maymunah's marriage, reports conflict on whether the Prophet married her while in ihram. The Hanafis noted that all reports agree the marriage was not contracted during the original state of ihram; the dispute is only about a secondary issue. They therefore preferred the negating report (that he was not muhrim) because it better aligns with the consensus that the marriage was valid. In the case of Zaynab, reports conflict on whether the Prophet returned her to her husband with a new marriage contract or retained the original. The Hanafis preferred the affirming report (new contract). These examples show that the rule 'al-muthbit awla' is the default, but it may yield to other considerations such as istihsan or ijmali agreement.

Key Terms
المثبت (al-muthbit): The report that affirms a change or new legal status beyond the original state
النافي (al-nafi): The report that denies any change and leaves the matter upon the original state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which report did the Hanafis prefer in the case of Barirah's husband's status?
A) The negating report (her husband was a slave)
B) The affirming report (her husband was free)
C) They considered both conflicting and did not decide
D) They rejected both reports

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule 'al-muthbit awla' is the default for conflicting reports, but it may be overridden by consensus or istihsan, as demonstrated in the Maymunah case.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on the Mechanism of Istithna'
Presents Hanafi and Shafi'i views on how istithna' operates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i views on how istithna' (exception) functions in legal speech, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) using the debt example.

Core Idea
Hanafis treat istithna' as reformulating the speech; Shafi'is treat it as opposing the ruling like a specificity sign.

This passage from a classical usul text explores a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the linguistic mechanism of exception (istithna'). Understanding this distinction is crucial because it affects how legal statements are interpreted, especially in contracts and obligations. The practical consequence, shown in the debt example, reveals the real impact of this usul principle on daily transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The dispute centers on how istithna' (exception) operates in a statement. Is it a modification of the speech itself or an opposition to the ruling? Pinpoint exactly what the two schools disagree about.
The issue is the mechanism (kayfiyyah) of istithna', not its validity.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafis (our companions), the exception prevents the speaker from having uttered the ruling for the excepted part. The speech is effectively about the remainder only.
Hanafis see istithna' as a linguistic tool that reformulates the original utterance.
3
State the Shafi'i position
According to al-Shafi'i, the exception opposes the ruling by way of mu'arada (opposition), similar to a dalil al-khusus (sign of specificity). It introduces a separate ruling that contradicts the general implication.
Shafi'is view istithna' as a normative contradiction, not a linguistic reformulation.
4
Analyze the practical example
Examine the debt statement: 'I owe you a thousand dirhams except one hundred.' Apply both views to see the different outcomes. According to Hanafis, the debtor owes nine hundred. According to Shafi'is, the excepted hundred is simply not owed (the obligation is for the remainder but the excepted part is excluded from the claim).
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: one restates the obligation; the other negates the obligation for the excepted part.
5
Connect to Quranic illustration
The text mentions the Shafi'i interpretation of Quran 2:160 ('except those who repent') to show how the exception introduces a new ruling (no flogging, acceptance of testimony) rather than just modifying the previous ruling. This illustrates the Shafi'i view.
Hanafis would interpret the verse differently, but the passage does not elaborate their counter-interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في كيفية عمل كل واحد منهما :
فقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله : الاستثناء يمنع التكلم بحكمه بقدر المستثنى، فيجعل تكلماً بالباقي بعده.
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : إن الاستثناء يمنع الحكم بطريق المعارضة، بمنزلة دليل الخصوص.
كما اختلفوا في التعليق على ما سبق ذكره، وقد دل على هذا الأصل مسائلهم.
فصار عندنا تقدير قول الرجل: لفلان علي ألف درهم إلا مائةً : لفلان علي تسعمائة.
وعنده إلا مائة : فإنها ليست علي.
وبيان ذلك : أنه جعل قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوا ﴾ البقرة / ١٦٠ : بمعنى قوله إلا الذين تابوا فلا تجلدوهم واقبلوا شهادتهم، وأولئك هم الصالحون، غير فاسقين.
And they differed concerning the mechanism of each of them: Our companions (Hanafis) said: Exception prevents the speaker from uttering the ruling to the extent of the excepted part, thus making him speak only of the remainder after it. And al-Shafi'i said: Exception prevents the ruling by way of opposition (mu'arada), like a sign of specificity (dalil al-khusus). Similarly they differed on suspension (ta'liq) as mentioned before, and their cases indicate this principle. Thus according to us, the estimation of a man's saying 'I owe so-and-so a thousand dirhams except one hundred' is 'I owe so-and-so nine hundred'. And according to him, 'except one hundred' means they are not owed to him. An illustration of that: He (al-Shafi'i) interprets the verse 'Except those who repent' (2:160) as meaning 'except those who repent, do not flog them and accept their testimony, and they are the righteous, not sinners.'
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents the usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on how istithna' (exception) functions. The Hanafis (as attributed to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the exception prevents the speaker from having actually spoken the ruling for the excepted part; hence the remaining speech is like saying only the remainder (e.g., 'nine hundred' instead of 'a thousand except one hundred'). The Shafi'i view (Imam al-Shafi'i) is that the exception opposes the ruling that would have applied, acting as a sign of specificity (dalil al-khusus) that contradicts the general implication. The text notes a similar disagreement on ta'liq (suspension) referenced earlier. It then illustrates the practical difference: for the statement 'I owe you a thousand dirhams except one hundred', the Hanafi interpretation is that the debtor owes nine hundred (the exception modifies the speech), while the Shafi'i interpretation is that the excepted part is simply not owed (the exception opposes the claim for that part). The Shafi'i proof is their understanding of Quranic verses like 'except those who repent' where the exception introduces a new ruling (no flogging, acceptance of testimony) rather than just negating the initial ruling.

Key Terms
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Opposition or contradiction; the way Shafi'is view istithna' as opposing the general ruling.
دليل الخصوص (dalil al-khusus): A sign indicating specificity, limiting a general statement; Shafi'is compare istithna' to this.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exception; a linguistic tool that excludes part of a general statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the text, what does the statement 'I owe you a thousand dirhams except one hundred' mean?
A) The excepted hundred is simply not owed, but the remaining is ambiguous.
B) The speaker owes nine hundred dirhams.
C) The speaker owes nothing because the exception invalidates the whole.
D) The speaker owes a thousand dirhams but the excepted hundred is a gift.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view treats istithna' as reframing the speech, while the Shafi'i view treats it as opposing the ruling; the practical difference is whether the excepted part is simply excluded or the obligation is restated.

Following the Prophet's Ambiguous Actions: A Hanafi Dispute
Presents differing views on whether to follow actions when their legal description is unclear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions on following the Prophet's actions when their legal description is ambiguous and apply the tariff preferred view in deriving rulings.

Core Idea
Following the Prophet's actions is the default; ambiguity triggers a three-way dispute on suspension vs. evidence-based following.

This lesson explores an internal Hanafi dispute on how to treat the Prophet's (ﷺ) actions when their legal description (sifa) is unclear. Three positions emerge: al-Jassas and al-Karkhi require following until evidence to the contrary, the Waqifun suspend judgment, and others invoke the principle of imamate. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to handle ambiguous prophetic actions in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Positions
Read the source text and list the three main views: (1) al-Jassas and al-Karkhi, (2) the Waqifun, (3) the 'others' who cite general obedience texts.
Note that the author aligns with al-Jassas and al-Karkhi.
2
Analyze Each Position's Dalil
For each group, extract the evidence they use. al-Jassas and al-Karkhi: certainty cannot be abandoned without proof; Waqifun: following requires conformity in essence and description; Others: Qur'anic commands to obey the Prophet and the principle of imamate.
The text provides a Qur'anic citation (24:63) and a prophetic analogy.
3
Evaluate the Tarjih
The author states that al-Jassas's view is 'more correct according to us'. Understand why: it balances following with the need for evidence to depart from certainty.
This is a methodological preference, not a dismissal of other views.
4
Consider the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: if you adopt the Waqifun position, you would not use doubtful actions as legal proofs until clarity; if you adopt the majority view, you follow such actions unless proven otherwise.
This directly impacts how one derives rulings from the Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الجصاص رحمه الله مثل قول الكرخي، إلا أنه قال: علينا اتباعه، لا نترك ذلك إلا بدليل. وهذا أصح عندنا. وأما الواقفون فقد قالوا: إن صفة الفعل إذا كانت مشكلة: امتنع الاقتداء به؛ لأن الاقتداء هو المتابعة في أصله ووصفه، فإذا خالفه في الوصف : لم يكن مقتدياً، فوجب الوقف إلى أن يظهر. وأما الآخرون فقد احتجوا بالنص الموجب لطاعة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام قال الله تعالى: ﴿ فَلْيَحْذَرِ الَّذِينَ يُخَالِفُونَ عَنْ أَمْرِ ا (النور / 63) والنصوص في ذلك كثيرة. وأما الكرخي الثابتة بيقين فلم يجز إثبات غيرها إلا بدليل، ووجب إثبات اليقين، كمن وكل رجلاً بماله يثبتُ الحفظ به؛ لأنه يقين، وقد وجدنا اختصاص الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ببعض ما فَعَلَه (1) ، ووجدنا الاشتراك أيضاً، (1) باب أفعال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قوله وقد وجدنا اختصاص الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ببعض ما فعله قال الشارح مثل العدد في النكاح، والصفي في المَعْنَم، وقيام الليل، والضُّحى. عن أنس بن مالك رضي الله عنه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يطوف على نسائه في الساعة من الليل والنهار وهنَّ إحدى عشرة». رواه البخاري والنسائي. وللبخاري في رواية: «وهن تسع نسوة». ووفق بأن الزائدتين : سريتان ذكرنا في النساء تغليباً. وعن قتادة قال: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا غزى بنفسه: يكون له فوجب الوقف فيه أيضاً. ووجه القول الآخر: أن الاتباع أصل؛ لأنه إمام يُقتدى به، كما قال تعالى لإبراهيم عليه السلام : ﴿وَإِنِّي جَاعِلُكَ لِلنَّاسِ إِمَامًا (البقرة / 124) فوجب التمسك بالأصل حتى يقوم الدليل على غيره.
Al-Jassas (may Allah have mercy on him) said like the statement of al-Karkhi, except that he said: It is incumbent upon us to follow him; we do not abandon that except with a proof. And this is more correct according to us. As for the Waqifun, they said: When the description (sifa) of an act is problematic, following it is prevented, because following is conformity in both its essence and description. So if one differs from it in description, he is not a follower, so suspension becomes obligatory until [the matter] becomes clear. As for the others, they argued with the text obligating obedience to the Messenger (peace be upon him). Allah Almighty said: 'So let those beware who dissent from his command' (Qur'an 24:63), and the texts on this are many. As for al-Karkhi, [that which is] established with certainty cannot be proven otherwise except with proof, and certainty must be established, like one who appoints an agent with his property—security (hifz) is established through him because it is certainty. And we have found the Messenger's exclusivity in some of his actions, and we have also found commonality. (Footnote: Section on the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him): His statement: 'And we have found the Messenger's exclusivity in some of his actions': The commentator said: Examples are the number in marriage, the safi in the booty, night prayer, and forenoon prayer. From Anas ibn Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to visit his wives in one hour of night and day, and they were eleven. Narrated by Bukhari and Nasa'i. And in a narration of Bukhari: 'and they were nine wives.' And it was reconciled that the extra two were concubines, mentioned among women by way of inclusion. And from Qatada: He said: When the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) himself raided, he had... So suspension became obligatory also. And the face of the other opinion: That following is the original principle, because he is an Imam to be followed, as Allah Almighty said to Ibrahim (peace be upon him): 'Indeed, I will make you a leader for the people' (Qur'an 2:124). So it is necessary to adhere to the original principle until proof arises for something else.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute among Hanafi scholars regarding the correct response to the Prophet's actions when their legal description (sifa) is ambiguous. It begins by citing al-Jassas, who agrees with al-Karkhi that we must follow the action but adds that we should not abandon it without evidence; the author considers this the most correct view. Next, the Waqifun (suspensionists) argue that if the description of an act is problematic (e.g., we do not know if it was obligatory, recommended, or permissible), following is prevented because true following requires conformity in both essence and description; thus one must suspend judgment until the matter becomes clear. The 'others' (likely those who advocate immediate following) cite the Quranic command to obey the Prophet (Qur'an 24:63) and other texts requiring obedience. Al-Karkhi's position is then explained: that which is established with certainty cannot be set aside except with proof, and certainty must be maintained. He draws an analogy: just as one who appoints an agent with his property must be sure of the agent's trustworthiness, so certainty in the Prophet's action must be preserved. The author notes that we find actions exclusive to the Prophet (e.g., marrying more than four wives, the safi—a special share of booty, night prayer, forenoon prayer) and actions shared with the ummah. The footnote provides examples and narrations. The passage concludes with a third opinion: that following is the default principle because the Prophet is an imam to be followed, as Allah said to Ibrahim (Qur'an 2:124); so we must adhere to following until evidence indicates otherwise.

Key Terms
الاقتداء (al-iqtida'): Following or imitating the Prophet in his actions, both in essence and description.
الوقف (al-waqf): Suspending judgment on the legal status of an action until it becomes clear.
الصفة (al-sifa): The legal description or attribute of an action (e.g., being obligatory, recommended, permissible).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Waqifun, what is the reason for suspending judgment on following the Prophet's action when its description is problematic?
A) Because the Prophet's actions are not a source of legislation.
B) Because following requires conformity in both essence and description, and differing in description negates following.
C) Because the action may be abrogated.
D) Because it is a matter of personal choice.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's preferred position is to follow the Prophet's actions unless clear evidence shows they were exclusive or not meant for the ummah, balancing certainty and the obligation of obedience.

The Binding Nature of Previous Sharia Laws
Introduces the chapter and states three opinions on previous laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scholarly positions on the binding nature of previous prophets' laws and identify the Hanafi position with its condition that the law must be narrated without disapproval.

Core Idea
Previous laws bind us only if Allah or His Messenger narrates them without disapproval, making them part of our sharia.

The question of whether the laws of earlier prophets are binding on the Muslim ummah is a classical usul al-fiqh debate. This passage from al-Bazdawi's Kanz al-Wusul introduces the chapter and outlines three distinct views. Understanding this dispute is crucial for knowing which pre-Islamic rulings remain applicable and how they relate to the finality of prophetic law.

Guided Path
1
Parse the three opinions
Identify the three positions: 1) all previous laws bind us unless abrogated; 2) none bind us unless proven; 3) only those narrated without disapproval bind us as our own law. Note that the third is the Hanafi position.
The author declares the third as 'al-sahih 'indana' (correct with us).
2
Identify the Hanafi condition
The Hanafi position restricts binding force to laws that Allah or His Messenger narrates to us without disapproval. This means the law must be 'adopted' into Islamic sharia by either affirmation or absence of rejection.
The condition is twofold: narration (qassahu) and absence of disapproval (min ghayri inkar).
3
Examine the first group's Qur'anic proof
The first group cites three verses: Al-An'am 6:90 (follow their guidance), Al-Baqarah 2:285 (no distinction between messengers), Al-Ma'idah 5:48 (confirming previous scripture). The operative phrase is 'huda' (guidance), which they argue includes laws.
The passage cuts off while presenting the second group's argument, so we focus on the proof provided.
4
Appreciate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a ruling from a previous prophet (e.g., the law of retaliation in the Torah) would be automatically binding under the first view, but under the Hanafi view it binds only if the Quran or Sunnah affirms it without disapproval.
This shows how the dispute affects the derivation of rulings from non-Qur'anic/Sunnah sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب شرائع من قبلنا. قال بعض العلماء: تلزمنا شرائعُ مَن قَبلنا حتى يقوم الدليل على النسخ، بمنزلة شرائعنا. وقال بعضهم : لا تلزمنا حتى يقوم الدليل. وقال بعضهم : تلزمنا على أنه شريعتنا. والصحيح عندنا : أن ما قص الله تعالى منها علينا من غير إنكار، أو قصه رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من غير إنكار : فإنه يلزمنا، على أنه شريعه رسولنا عليه الصلاة والسلام. احتج الأولون بقوله تبارك وتعالى: (أُوْلَيْكَ الَّذِينَ هَدَى اللَّهُ فَهُدَهُمُ اقتادة ( الأنعام / ۹۰ ، والهدى : اسم يقع على الإيمان والشرائع. ولأنه ثبت حقيته دينا الله تبارك وتعالى ودين الله تعالى حَسَنٌ مرضي عنده، قال الله تبارك وتعالى: لَا نُفَرِّقُ بَيْنَ أَحَدٍ مِّن رُّسُلِهِ ، البقرة / ٢٨٥ ، وقال : مُصَدِّقًا لِمَا بَيْنَ يَدَيْهِ مِنَ الْكِتَبِ وَمُهَيْمِنًا عَلَيْهِ المائدة / ٤٨. فصار الأصل : هو الموافقة. واحتج أهل المقالة الثانية : بقول الله تبارك وتعالى: ﴿ لِكُلِّ جَعَلْنَا
Chapter: Laws of those before us. Some scholars said: The laws of those before us are binding upon us until evidence of abrogation appears, like our own laws. And some said: They are not binding until evidence appears. And some said: They are binding as our own law. And the correct according to us is: That which Allah Almighty narrated to us without disapproval, or which the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) narrated without disapproval, is binding upon us, as the law of our Messenger (peace be upon him). The first group argued with His saying, Blessed and Exalted: 'Those are the ones whom Allah has guided, so follow their guidance' (Al-An'am 6:90), and guidance is a term that includes faith and laws. And because it is established that it is the religion of Allah, and the religion of Allah is good and pleasing to Him. Allah said: 'We make no distinction between any of His messengers' (Al-Baqarah 2:285), and He said: 'Confirming what was before it of the Scripture and as a guardian over it' (Al-Ma'idah 5:48). So the principle is agreement. And the second group argued with His saying: 'For each We have appointed...'
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh discussion on whether the sharia (laws) of previous prophets are binding on the followers of Prophet Muhammad (saw). The text presents three scholarly opinions. The first opinion holds that all previous laws are binding unless abrogated, just like our own laws. The second holds that they are not binding at all. The third, which is the preferred Hanafi position, holds that only those laws that Allah or His Messenger narrated to us without disapproval are binding, and they become part of our sharia. The author then begins to provide the proof for the first group, citing Quranic verses (Al-An'am 6:90, Al-Baqarah 2:285, Al-Ma'idah 5:48) to argue that since guidance includes all laws and Allah approves of them, and since we must follow the guidance of previous prophets, their laws are binding. The argument is that there is a principle of agreement (muwafaqah). The text then starts to present the proof of the second group but cuts off.

Key Terms
شرائع من قبلنا (shara'i' man qablana): The religious laws and codes that were revealed to prophets before Prophet Muhammad (saw).
الهدى (al-huda): Guidance; in this context, it encompasses both faith (iman) and the laws (shara'i').
شريعة (shari'ah): A divine law or code; specifically, the law of a particular prophet.
إنكار (inkar): Disapproval; the condition that the narrated law must not be rejected or disapproved by Allah or His Messenger.

D) They are binding only if they were revealed to a prophet mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that previous prophets' laws bind Muslims only when they are narrated without disapproval, thus becoming part of the sharia of Prophet Muhammad (saw).

Dealing with Disagreement Among Companions and Following Tabi'is
Rules for resolving Companion ikhtilaf and conditions for following Tabi'is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for resolving ikhtilaf among Companions and identify when a Tabi'i's opinion can be followed.

Core Idea
When Companions disagree without a marfu' hadith, their statements are like conflicting qiyas; a Tabi'i may be followed only if he fatwaed in their era.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul establishes two key rules: the method for preferring one Companion's opinion over another when they conflict, and the conditions under which a Tabi'i (Follower) can be treated like a Companion in legal authority. These rules are foundational for the Hanafi approach to ikhtilaf and taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main issues
The passage covers Companion ikhtilaf and Tabi'i authority. Recognize each part.
2
Understand the rule for Companion ikhtilaf
When no marfu' hadith is involved, their statements are like conflicting qiyas; tarjih is required, and if impossible, the mujtahid may choose any one, but only one is correct.
This is based on the removal of tawqif possibility.
3
Grasp the condition for following a Tabi'i
A Tabi'i who did not issue fatwas in the Companion era cannot be followed; if he did, there is ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars.
Some treat him like a Companion; others say his taqlid is invalid.
4
Note the proof for the first opinion on Tabi'i
The example of Shuraih disagreeing with Ali shows that Tabi'is can be considered like Companions when they participated in ijtihad during that era.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما إذا اختلفوا في شيء : فإن الحق في أقوالهم، لا يعدوهم عندنا، على ما نبين في باب الإجماع إن شاء الله تعالى. ولا يسقط البعض بالبعض بالتعارض؛ لأنهم لما اختلفوا، ولم تَجْرِ المحاجة بالحديث المرفوع سقط احتمال التوقيف، وتعين وجه الرأي والاجتهاد، فصار تعارض أقوالهم كتعارض وجوه القياس، وذلك يوجب الترجيح، فإن تعذر الترجيح وجب العمل بأيها شاء المجتهد على أن الصواب واحد منها ، لا غير. ثم لا يجوز العمل بالباقي من بعد إلا بدليل، على ما مرَّ في باب المعارضة. متابعة أقوال التابعين : ] وأما التابعي فإن كان لم يبلغ درجة الفتوى في زمن الصحابة، ولم یزاحمهم في الرأي كان أسوة سائر أئمة الفتوى من السلف، لا يصح تقليده. وإن ظهرت فتواه في زمن الصحابة كان مثلهم في هذا الباب عند بعض مشايخنا رحمهم الله ؛ لتسليمهم مزاحمته إياهم. وقال بعضُهم بل لا يصح تقليده، وهو دونهم؛ لعدم احتمال التوقيف فيه. ووجه القول الأول: أن شريحاً خالف علياً رضي الله عنه عياناً في ردّ
As for when they (the Companions) disagree on something: then the truth is in their opinions; it does not go beyond them according to us, as we will explain in the chapter of consensus if Allah wills. And one does not cancel another due to contradiction; because when they disagreed and the debate did not involve a marfu' hadith, the possibility of tawqif falls and the aspect of opinion and ijtihad is determined. So their conflicting statements become like conflicting aspects of qiyas, and that necessitates tarjih (preference). If tarjih is impossible, then it is obligatory to act with whichever the mujtahid chooses, on the basis that the correct is one of them, no other. Then it is not permissible to act with the remaining after that except with proof, as has passed in the chapter of opposition. Following the statements of the Followers: As for the Tabi'i (Follower), if he had not reached the level of fatwa in the time of the Companions and did not compete with them in opinion, he is like other imams of fatwa among the Salaf; his taqlid is not valid. But if his fatwa appeared in the time of the Companions, then he is like them in this matter according to some of our mashayikh; because they accepted his competition with them. And some said: rather, his taqlid is not valid, and he is inferior to them due to the lack of possibility of tawqif in him. And the proof of the first opinion: that Shuraih openly disagreed with Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) in the case of returning...
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: first, how to deal with differences among the Companions. According to the Hanafis, the truth is confined to their opinions, none of them can be dismissed simply because others contradict it. When there is no explicit marfu' hadith presented in the debate, the tawqif factor is removed, and their statements are treated like conflicting analogies, requiring tarjih (preference). If tarjih is impossible, a mujtahid may choose any one, but only one is correct. Subsequent action on the other is not allowed without new evidence. Second, the status of the Tabi'in (Followers): a Follower who did not issue fatwas in the time of the Companions is like any other scholar; his taqlid is not permissible (like any non-mujtahid). However, if he gave fatwas during the Companion's era and they accepted his competition, some Hanafi scholars consider him like a Companion in authority, but others reject his taqlid because he lacks the possibility of direct tawqif. The first view is supported by the example of Shuraih who openly disagreed with Ali in a case (likely the story of Shuraih's judgement or disagreement regarding returning a gift or similar).

Key Terms
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): The process of preferring one evidence over another when they conflict, based on strength of proof.
التابعي (al-tābiʿī): A Follower who met the Companions and learned from them but is not a Companion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must have met at least 10 Companions.
B) He must have issued fatwas during the Companion era.
C) He must be from the first generation of Tabi'in.
D) He must be a scholar of Kufa.

Key Takeaway
When Companions disagree without a marfu' hadith, their opinions are like conflicting qiyas; a Tabi'i can be followed only if he fatwaed in the Companion era, and even then scholars differ.

Dispute on Validating the ‘Illah: Ittirād vs. Meaningful Cause
Dispute on conditions for valid legal cause in qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi conditions for a valid 'illah (legal cause) and contrast them with the Ahl al-Tard position, and apply the Hanafi standard to evaluate proposed causes.

Core Idea
A valid ‘illah requires a meaningful connection and a shar‘ī effect; mere consistency (ittirād) is insufficient.

In this lesson, we examine a key usul dispute over what makes a legal cause (‘illah) valid. The Ahl al-Tard argue that a description becomes a cause simply by being consistently co-extensive with the ruling. The Hanafis, as represented by the imams of fiqh, insist on an intelligible meaning (ma‘nā ma‘qūl) that has a real effect in the Shari‘ah. The passage then unpacks two sub-conditions: ṣalāḥ (suitability) and ‘adālah (validity), and explains how the Hanafi school defines ‘adālah as having an effect (athar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed premise
Recognise that not every attribute of a text can be taken as a valid cause – there is a selection requirement.
The passage starts with this unanimous point.
2
State the two conflicting views
Contrast the Ahl al-Tard (consistency suffices) with the Imams of fiqh (meaningful cause required).
The Imams include the Hanafi school.
3
Explain the Hanafi requirement of ma‘nā ma‘qūl
Understand that the cause must have an intelligible meaning, analogised to a witness who must be sound and qualified.
The analogy is detailed in the text.
4
Break down the two conditions: ṣalāḥ and ‘adālah
Ṣalāḥ is suitability to transmitted causes from the Salaf; ‘adālah, according to Hanafis, is the cause having a shar‘ī effect (athar).
These are necessary for the cause to be a proof.
5
Compare the Shāfi‘ī views on ‘adālah
Some Shāfi‘īs define ‘adālah as being suggestive (mukhayyil) and requiring examination against principles, while others require examination before acting.
The Hanafi position is distinct in focusing on actual effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتفقوا أن كل أوصاف النص بجملتها لا يجوز أن تكون علة.
واختلفوا في دلالة كونه علة على قولين:
فقال أهل الطرد: إنه يصير حجة بمجرد الاطراد، من غير معنى يعقل. وقال أئمة الفقه من السلف والخلف رحمهم الله: إنه لا حجة إلا يصير بمعنى يعقل، وهذا المعنى هو صلاح الوصف، ثم عدالته. وذلك على مثال الشاهد، لا بد من صلاحه بما يصير به منه أداء الشهادة، ثم لا يصح الأداء إلا بلفظ الشهادة، ثم عدالته؛ ليصح أهلا.
واتفقوا في صلاحه أنه إنما يراد به ملاءمته، وذلك أن يكون على موافقة ما جاء عن السلف رضي الله عنهم من العلل المنقولة؛ لأنه أمر شرعي، فيتعرف منه ولا يصح العمل به قبل الملاءمة، كما لا العمل بشهادة الشاهد قبل الأهلية، لكن لا يجب العمل به إلا بعد العدالة. والعدالة عندنا الأثر، وإنما نعني بالأثر: ما جعل له أثر في الشرع. وقال بعض أصحاب الشافعي رحمه الله: عدالته بكونه مخيلاً، ثم العرض على الأصول احتياطاً؛ لسلامته عن المناقضة والمعارضة.
وقال بعض أصحاب الشافعي رحمه الله: بل عدالته بالعرض على الأصول، فإن لم يرده أصل مناقض ولا معارض صار معدلاً، وإنما يعرض على أصلين فصاعداً.
فعلى القول الأول: يصح العمل به قبل العرض، وعلى الثاني: لا.
And they agreed that all the attributes of the text in their entirety cannot be a cause. And they differed concerning the indication of its being a cause on two statements: The people of consistency said: it becomes a proof by mere consistency, without an intelligible meaning. And the imams of fiqh from the predecessors and successors, may Allah have mercy on them, said: it is not a proof unless it becomes a proof by an intelligible meaning, and this meaning is the soundness of the description, then its validity. And that is on the example of the witness: there must be his soundness by which he is fit to perform testimony, then the performance is not valid except with the wording of testimony, then his validity so that he is qualified. And they agreed concerning its soundness that what is intended by it is its suitability, and that is that it conforms to what has come from the predecessors, may Allah be pleased with them, of transmitted causes, because it is a legal matter, so it is known from it, and it is not valid to act upon it before suitability, just as the testimony of a witness is not acted upon before eligibility, but it is not obligatory to act upon it except after validity. And validity according to us is the effect, and we mean by effect: that which has an effect in the law. And some of the followers of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: its validity is by being suggestive, then examination by the principles as a precaution, so that it is safe from contradiction and opposition. And some of the followers of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: rather its validity is by examination by the principles, and if no principle contradicts or opposes it, it becomes valid, and it is examined by two or more principles. According to the first statement: it is valid to act upon it before examination, and according to the second: not.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute about the condition for an 'illah (legal cause) to be valid. It begins with an agreed principle: not every attribute of a text can be taken as a cause. Then the dispute is introduced: (1) The 'Ahl al-Tard' (proponents of consistency or ittirād) hold that a cause becomes a proof simply by being consistently co-extensive with the ruling, without needing an intelligible meaning. (2) The 'A'immat al-Fiqh' (the leading jurists, including Hanafis) hold that a cause requires an intelligible meaning (ma'na ma'qul), which consists of two conditions: salah (soundness/fitness) and 'adalah (validity). The author draws an analogy with a witness: the witness must be sound (fit to testify) and then must pronounce the testimony, and then be 'adil (upright) to be qualified. Similarly, for the 'illah, 'salah' means suitability—the cause must conform with transmitted causes from the Salaf, because it is a shar'i matter. Acting upon it before establishing suitability is invalid, just as a witness's testimony is not accepted before eligibility. 'Adalah (validity) according to the Hanafis is 'athar'—meaning the cause must have a real effect in the Shari'ah. Some Shafi'is define validity as being suggestive (mukhayyil), and then requiring examination of the principles (usul) to ensure no contradiction. Another Shafi'i view says validity is achieved only after examining against two or more principles. According to the first view, the cause can be acted upon before such examination; according to the second, it cannot. This highlights the Hanafi emphasis on substantive effect over mere consistency.

Key Terms
الاطراد (al-ittirād): Consistency of a description with the ruling; the cause being co-extensive without counterexample.
صلاح الوصف (ṣalāḥ al-waṣf): soundness of the description, meaning it must conform to transmitted causes from the Salaf.
عدالة الوصف (‘adālat al-waṣf): validity of the description; in Hanafi view, having an effect (athar) in shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi definition of ‘adālah?
A) Mere consistency (ittirād)
B) Being suggestive (mukhayyil)
C) Having an effect (athar) in the Shari‘ah
D) Examination against two or more principles

Key Takeaway
A valid ‘illah in Hanafi usul requires both suitability to transmitted causes and a real legal effect, not mere consistency.

Refuting the Anger Hadith as a Valid Illah
Refuting an opposing view on the angry judge hadith as illa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of using the hadith 'لا يقضي القاضي وهو غضبان' as an illah for qiyas and explain why the Hanafi school relies on explicit textual indication (dalalat al-nass) over subjective states.

Core Idea
A ruling's cause must be consistent and textually grounded; subjective states like anger cannot form a universal illah.

In this passage, the author refutes an attempt to use the well-known hadith about the angry judge as a general cause (illah) for invalidating acts due to preoccupation. The Hanafi position insists that rulings, especially in matters of worship, must be derived from explicit textual evidence (dalalat al-nass) rather than rare psychological states. By analyzing the Quranic verses on wudu and ghusl, the author demonstrates the correct method of istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing argument
The opponent uses the hadith 'The judge should not judge while angry' to argue that anger (as a form of preoccupation) invalidates judgment, and by analogy, any preoccupation invalidates acts.
The hadith is cited as a potential illah for nullifying acts due to mental distraction.
2
Note the Hanafi critique
The Hanafi scholar points out that the condition in the hadith—anger with an empty heart—is extremely rare, making it unsuitable as a universal principle for qiyas.
Rarity undermines the consistency required for a valid illah.
3
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The author turns to the Quranic verses about wudu and ghusl (Al-Ma'idah 5:6) to show that rulings are based on explicit textual indicators (hadath) rather than subjective states.
The Quran serves as the primary text for deriving conditions of purity.
4
Analyse the textual derivation
The Hanafi explanation interprets 'qumtum' (when you rise) as a metaphor for sleep, which indicates hadath; the structure of tayammum as a substitute further confirms that hadath is the operative cause.
This demonstrates how dalalat al-nass operates in Hanafi usul.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school prioritizes explicit textual evidence over weak analogies based on rare conditions, thus refuting the opposing use of the hadith as an illah.
Causation (ta'lil) is only for extension, not for establishing original rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو غضبان: أنه معلول بشغل القلب؛ لأنه يحل له القضاء وهو غضبان عند فراغ القلب، ولا يحل القضاء عند شغله بغير الغضب، إلا أن هذا شرط لا يكاد يوجد إلا نادراً في بعض الأصول ظاهراً، فكيف يُجعل أصلا؟! وذلك غيرُ مسلَّم أيضاً؛ لأن الحَدَثَ لم يثبت في باب الوضوء بالتعليل، بل بدلالة النص وصيغته. أما الصيغة: فلأنه ذَكَرَ التيمم بالتراب الذي هو بدل عن معلقاً بالحدث. وكذلك ذَكَرَ الغُسل، وهو أعظمُ الطُّهرين، فقال جل ذكره: {وَإِن كُنتُمْ جُنَّبًا فَأَطَهَرُوا} [المائدة/6]، وقال تعالى: {وَإِن كُنتُم مَرْضَى أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ أَوْ جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِنكُم مِّنَ الْغَابِطِ أَوْ لَمَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَلَمْ تَجِدُوا مَاءً فَتَيَمَّمُوا} [المائدة/6]. والنص في البدل نص في الأصل؛ لأنه يفارقه بحاله، لا بسببه. وأما الدلالة: فقوله تعالى: {إِذَا قُمْتُمْ إِلَى الصَّلوة} [المائدة/6]: أي من مضاجعكم، وهو كناية عن النوم، والنوم دليل الحدث، وهذا النظم والله أعلم؛ لأن الوضوء مطهر، فدل على قيام النجاسة، فاستغني عن ذكره، بخلاف التيمم. والوضوء متعلق بالصلاة، والحدث شرطه، فلم يُذكر الحدث؛ ليُعلم أنه سنة أو فرض، فكان الحدث شرطاً؛ لكونه فرضاً، لا لكونه سنة. فأما الغُسل: فلا يُس لكل صلاة، بل هو فرض خالص، فلم يُشرع إلا مقروناً بالحدث. وكذلك الغضب معلول بشغل القلب، وقطّ لا يوجد غضب بلا شغل قلب، ولا يحل القضاء إلا بعد سكونه، وإنما التعليل للتعدية.
And he is angry: that it is caused by preoccupation of the heart; because it is permissible for him to judge while angry when the heart is empty, and it is not permissible to judge when it is occupied with something other than anger, except that this is a condition that hardly exists except rarely in some apparent principles, so how can it be made a principle?! That is also not conceded, because hadath (ritual impurity) was not established in the chapter of ablution by causation, but by the indication of the text and its wording. As for the wording: because He mentioned tayammum with earth, which is a substitute for that which is conditioned by hadath. Similarly, He mentioned ghusl, which is the greater of the two purifications, and He, exalted be His mention, said: {And if you are junub, then purify yourselves} [Al-Ma'idah/6], and He said: {And if you are ill or on a journey or one of you comes from the toilet or you have touched women and you find no water, then perform tayammum} [Al-Ma'idah/6]. And the text regarding the substitute is a text regarding the original, because it differs from it in its state, not in its cause. As for the indication: His statement {When you rise for prayer} [Al-Ma'idah/6]: meaning from your sleeping places, which is a metaphor for sleep, and sleep is evidence of hadath, and this arrangement, and Allah knows best, because ablution is a purifier, so it indicates the lifting of impurity, so its mention was dispensed with, unlike tayammum. And ablution is connected to prayer, and hadath is its condition, so hadath was not mentioned, so that it would be known whether it is sunnah or obligatory, so hadath is a condition because it is obligatory, not because it is sunnah. As for ghusl: it is not legislated for every prayer, but it is purely obligatory, so it was only legislated in conjunction with hadath. Similarly, anger is caused by preoccupation of the heart, and anger never occurs without preoccupation of the heart, and judgment is not permissible except after its calm, and causation is only for extension.
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument that uses the hadith 'The judge should not judge while angry' as an illah (cause) for invalidating judgments due to any preoccupation. The author (presumably a Hanafi scholar) argues that the condition of anger with an empty heart is extremely rare and cannot serve as a universal principle. He then provides textual evidence from the Quran regarding wudu and ghusl to show that rulings are derived from explicit textual indications, not from subjective states like anger. The author emphasizes that the Quran mentions tayammum as a substitute for water when hadath (impurity) is present, and that the word 'qumtum' (you rise) is a metaphor for sleep, which indicates hadath. The conclusion is that causation (ta'lil) is only for extension (ta'diyah), not for establishing original rulings.

Key Terms
العلة (al-illah): The cause or effective reason that extends a ruling from an original case to a subsidiary case.
التعليل (al-ta'lil): The process of identifying and applying the illah to extend a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadith is weak in transmission
B) Because the condition (preoccupation) rarely occurs in the specified form
C) Because the Quran explicitly contradicts it
D) Because the hadith applies only to judges

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school insists that illah must be reliable and textually anchored; a rare condition cannot form the basis of qiyas.

Why Negation Cannot Prove a Ruling
Classification and critique of argument types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a negation-based argument (ta'lil bil-nafy) and critique its validity using the Hanafi usul principle that negation alone does not prove affirmation.

Core Idea
Negation is nothing — it cannot serve as a proof for any positive ruling.

In this passage from Kanz al-Wusul, the author classifies types of legal arguments and then critiques the use of negation (adam) as a cause. You will learn why Hanafi usul rejects arguments like 'marriage is not proven by women's testimony because it is not wealth' as valid proofs, and how to distinguish pattern from substance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument type: negation vs. positive cause
Read the Shafi'i examples and determine whether the alleged 'illah (cause) is a positive attribute or a negation (like 'it is not wealth').
Negation-based arguments appear as causes but are actually 'adam (non-existence).
2
Understand the Hanafi classification of arguments
Review the seven divisions listed: ittiraad, istishab al-hal, ta'arud al-ashbah, etc. Note that negation arguments are not among the valid categories.
The author treats negation under the seventh category: 'argument by there being no proof'.
3
Analyze the critique of consistency (ittiraad)
Examine why ittiraad (consistency) alone is not a valid proof: frequency does not guarantee causation. The author uses the analogy that existence of a thing is not a cause for its own continuance.
This is a prelude to the main critique of negation.
4
Examine the Shafi'i examples of negation
List the three examples: marriage testimony, brother's emancipation, and divorce after permanent separation. For each, identify the negation used as the supposed cause.
These arguments are 'on the pattern of causes' but are actually negations.
5
Apply the Hanafi principle that negation is not a valid proof
State: Because negation is nothing (la shay'), it cannot serve as a proof for affirmation. The only exception is when the ruling is established by consensus and the negation is the sole possible cause.
The exception: Muhammad's ruling on usurped offspring — the negation is valid because it is based on consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوضوء متعلق بالصلاة، والحدث شرطه، فلم يُذكر الحدث؛ ليُعلم أنه سنة أو فرض (١) ، فكان الحدث شرطاً؛ لكونه فرضاً، لا لكونه سنة. فأما الغُسل : فلا يُس لكل صلاة ، بل هو فرض خالص، فلم يُشرع إلا مقروناً بالحدث. وكذلك الغضب معلول بشغل القلب، وقطّ لا يوجد غضب بلا شغل قلب، ولا يحل القضاء إلا بعد سكونه، وإنما التعليل للتعدية. وأما تقسيم هذه الجملة : ١ - فإن أول أقسامه : الاطراد، وجوداً، أو وجوداً وعدماً. ٢- والذي يليه : الاحتجاج باستصحاب الحال(۲). والذي يليه : الاحتجاج بتعارض الأشباه. ٤ والذي يليه : الاحتجاج بما لا يستقل إلا بوصف يقع به الفرق. ه والذي يليه : الاحتجاج بأن يكون الوصف مختلفاً ظاهر الاختلاف. ٦- والذي يليه : ما لا يُشك في فساده. - والذي يليه الاحتجاج بأن لا دليل. أما الأول : فلأن الاطرادَ لا يثبت به إلا كثرة الشهود، أو كثرة أداء الشهادة، وصحة الشهادة لا تُعرف بكثرة العدد ولا بتكرير العبارة، بل بأهلية الشاهد وعدالته واختصاص أدائه ولأن الوجود قد يكون اتفاقاً، والعدمَ قد يقع ؛ لأنه شرط. ألا يُرى أن وجودَ الشيء ليس بعلة لبقائه، فكيف يصلح علةً للوجود في غيره بنفسه ؟! وكذلك وجودُ الحكم ولا علة لا يصلح دليلاً لجواز وجوده بغيره، ووجود العلة ولا حكم بنفسه : لا يصلح مناقضاً؛ لجواز أن يقف الحكم لفَوْت وصف من العلة ليس بعلة بنفسه، فلا يكون مناقضة، ولا ذكره وقد دل عليه التعليل تخصيصاً، على ما نُبيّن إن شاء الله تعالى، إلا أن هذا على نهج العلل ظاهراً، فكان مقدماً في أقسامه، ثم التعليل بالنفي. مثل قول الشافعي رحمه الله في النكاح لا يثبت بشهادة النساء الرجال ؛ لأنه ليس بمال. وفي الأخ: لا يعتق؛ لأنه ليس بينهما بعضية. ولا يلحق المبتوتة طلاق؛ لأنه لا نكاح بينهما. مع ويجوز إسلام المروي في المَرْوي؛ لأنهما مالان لا يجمعهما طُعم ولا ثمنية وهذا في الظاهر خرج على مثال العلل، لكنه لما كان عدماً : لم يكن شيئاً، فلا يصلح حجةً للإثبات. ألا ترى أن استقصاء العدم : لا يمنعُ الوجود من وجه آخر إلا أن يقع الاختلافُ في حكم بسبب معين. وفي حكم ثبت دليله بالإجماع ،واحداً، لا ثاني له، مثل قول محمد رحمه الله في ولد الغصب ؛ لأنه لم يغصب الولد.
And wudu is related to prayer, and hadath is its condition; it was not mentioned [that it is] so that it be known that it is sunna or fard, so hadath became a condition because it is fard, not because it is sunna. As for ghusl: it is not for every prayer, rather it is pure fard, so it was not legislated except linked with hadath. Likewise, anger is caused by preoccupation of the heart, and anger absolutely does not exist without heart preoccupation, nor is judgment permissible until after its calmness, and the causation (ta'lil) is only for transference (ta'diya). As for the division of this statement: 1 - Its first divisions: consistency (ittiraad), in existence, or in both existence and non-existence. 2 - After it: argument by presumption of continuity (istishab al-hal). After it: argument by conflict of similarities (ta'arud al-ashbah). 4 - After it: argument by what cannot stand alone except with a description that differentiates. 5 - After it: argument by the description being apparently different. 6 - After it: what is undoubtedly corrupt. - After it: argument by there being no proof. As for the first: because consistency is only established by multitude of witnesses, or multitude of performance of testimony, and the validity of testimony is not known by multitude of number nor by repetition of expression, but by the qualification of the witness, his justice, and the specificity of his performance, and because existence may be coincidental, and non-existence may occur because it is a condition. Do you not see that the existence of a thing is not a cause for its continuance, so how can it be valid as a cause for existence in another thing by itself?! Likewise, the existence of a ruling without a cause is not valid as proof for the permissibility of its existence by something else, and the existence of a cause without a ruling by itself is not valid as a contradiction; because it is possible that the ruling is stopped due to the loss of an attribute of the cause that itself is not a cause, so there is no contradiction, nor its mention, though causation (ta'lil) has indicated it by specification, as we will explain, God willing, except that this is on the pattern of causes apparently, so it is advanced in its divisions, then causation by negation. Such as the statement of al-Shafi'i (may God have mercy on him) concerning marriage: it is not established by the testimony of women with men because it is not wealth. And concerning a brother: he is not freed because there is no sonship between them. And a permanently divorced woman is not affected by divorce because there is no marriage between them. Also, it is permissible to exchange the sold item for its price because they are two wealths not united by value or currency, and this in appearance comes out on the pattern of causes, but since it is negation, it is nothing, so it is not valid as a proof for affirmation. Do you not see that exhaustive enumeration of negation does not prevent existence from another aspect unless the dispute occurs about a specific ruling, and about a ruling whose proof is established by consensus, one, without second, such as the statement of Muhammad (may God have mercy on him) concerning the offspring of usurpation: because he did not usurp the child.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage is an usul discussion classifying types of arguments (hujaj) and critiquing the use of negation (adam) as a valid proof. The author begins with an example from fiqh: wudu is linked to prayer, and its condition (hadath) is not mentioned in the text to show that wudu can be either sunna or fard; however, hadath itself is a condition only when wudu is fard. For ghusl, which is always fard, it is only legislated together with hadath. Then, anger is used as an analogy: it is caused by heart preoccupation, and a judge cannot rule until the anger subsides. The author then lists seven categories of arguments: 1) ittiraad (consistency), 2) istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity), 3) ta'arud al-ashbah (conflict of similarities), 4) an argument that requires a distinguishing description, 5) an argument where the description is clearly different, 6) what is undoubtedly corrupt, and 7) argument that there is no proof. The explanation focuses on the first: ittiraad (consistency) is not a valid proof by itself because it only shows frequency, not reliability. Reliability depends on the witness's qualification and specificity, not numbers. Similarly, existence of a thing is not a cause for its own continuance, so it cannot be a cause for something else. The presence of a ruling without a cause does not prove it can exist by another cause, and the presence of a cause without a ruling is not contradictory because there may be a missing attribute. The author then critiques arguments based on negation (ta'lil bil-nafy), giving examples from al-Shafi'i: marriage is not proven by women's testimony because it is not wealth; a brother is not freed because no sonship; a permanently divorced woman does not get a new divorce because no marriage. These arguments appear to follow the pattern of causes but are actually negations, which do not prove affirmation. The author emphasizes that exhaustive negation does not prevent existence from another source unless there is a specific ruling proven by consensus, as in Imam Muhammad's ruling that the offspring of usurpation is not considered usurped because the usurper did not usurp the child. Thus, the Hanafi position is that negation alone is insufficient as a legal proof; only positive causes (ta'lil ithbati) are valid.

Key Terms
الاطراد (al-ittiraad): Consistency or regularity in the occurrence of a description with a ruling; it is the first division of arguments but does not establish a valid cause by itself.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity; arguing that a state or ruling remains as it was in the absence of evidence of change. It is the second division of arguments.
تعارض الأشباه (ta'arud al-ashbah): Conflict of similarities; when a case resembles two different principles equally, requiring further analysis. It is the third division of arguments.
التعليل بالنفي (al-ta'lil bil-nafy): Causation by negation; using a negative attribute as a legal cause (e.g., 'because it is not wealth').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the argument by negation (like 'it is not wealth') not a valid proof for affirming a ruling?
A) Because negation is nothing and thus cannot establish a positive ruling.
B) Because the number of witnesses is insufficient.
C) Because the description is not consistent (muttarid).
D) Because it is based on custom ('urf).

Key Takeaway
Negation-based arguments (ta'lil bil-nafy) are invalid in Hanafi usul because negation has no reality; only positive attributes can serve as legal causes.
