Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Istishab al-Hal as a Proof
Dispute: validity of istishab as a proof for obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis treat istishab as a defensive proof (hujja dafi'a) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using examples from sulh, shuf'a, and conditional manumission.

Core Idea
Istishab defends the status quo but cannot impose a new obligation — it is a shield, not a sword.

This passage presents a foundational usul dispute: can the presumption of continuity (istishab al-hal) be used to establish new obligations, or does it merely defend an existing state? The Hanafi school limits its role to defense, while Imam al-Shafi'i grants it broader authority. Understanding this difference clarifies several practical rulings across fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on istishab
Identify that Hanafis consider istishab a defensive proof (hujja dafi'a), not a proof that creates an obligation (hujja mujiba).
The text explicitly says: 'Upon us this is not a proof for obligation, but a defensive proof.'
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that istishab is a valid proof that can establish an obligation, based on the idea that a ruling established by its proof remains by that same proof.
He cites the permanence of Qur'anic rulings after the Prophet's death and the wudu example.
3
Examine the examples given for each position
Work through the three examples: sulh ala al-inkar, shuf'a, and conditional manumission. Note how each school rules differently based on their view of istishab.
The examples show the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal to the Shafi'i proof
The Hanafi response argues that the cause that establishes a ruling does not necessitate its continuation, just as creation does not imply continuance. Abrogation is possible.
The rebuttal uses the analogy of existence and non-existence not causing each other.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf for each example
Summarize the practical difference: sulh ala al-inkar is permissible for Hanafis, invalid for Shafi'is; shuf'a requires evidence for Hanafis, not for Shafi'is; in conditional manumission, the master's word is taken for Hanafis.
This highlights the importance of the usul principle in actual fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتجاج باستصحاب الحال فصحيح عند الشافعي رحمه الله، وذلك في كل حكم ثبت وعُرف وجوبه بدليله، ثم وقع زواله كان استصحاب حال البقاء على ذلك موجباً يصح الاحتجاج به على الخصم. وعندنا: هذا لا يكون حجةً للإيجاب، لكنها حجة دافعة، على ذلك دلَّت مسائلُهم، فقد قلنا في الصلح على الإنكار: إنه جائز. ولم نجعل براءة الذمة وهي أصل حجةً على المدعي، بل صار قول المدعي معارضاً لقوله على السواء. والشافعي رحمه الله جعله موجباً حتى تعدى إلى المدعي فأبطل دعواه، وأبطل الصلح. وقلنا في الشقص إذا بيع من الدار، فطلب الشريك الشفعة، فأنكر ملك الطالب فيما في يده: إن القول قوله، فلا تجب الشفعة إلا ببينة. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : تجب بغير بينة. وكذلك رجلٌ قال لعبده : إن لم تدخل الدار اليوم فأنت حر، فمضى اليوم ، ولا يُدرى أدخل أم لا ، ثم اختلفا فإن القول قول المولى عندنا ؛ لما ذكرنا. واحتج بأن الحكم إذا ثبت بدليله : بقي بذلك الدليل أيضاً، ألا يُرى أن حكم النص يبقى به بعد وفاة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام حتى تعذر نسخه. واحتج بإجماعهم على أن من تيقّن بالوضوء: لم يلزمه وضوء آخر، ولزمه أداء الصلاة بما علمه وإن شك في الحدث. وإذا علم بالحدث، ثم شك في الوضوء: بقي الحدث. ولو ثبت ملك الشفيع بإقرار المشتري أنه كان له، أو أنه اشتراه من فلان وفلانٌ كان يملكه : وجبت الشفعة، وإنما يبقى ملكه لعدم ما يُزيله ، ومع ذلك قد صلح حجةً موجبة. وكذلك لو شهد شهود المدعي أن هذا الشي كان ملكاً له: صار حجةً موجبة. ولنا أن الدليل الموجب لحكم لا يوجب بقاءه، كالإيجاد لا يوجبُ البقاء، حتى صح الإفناء، وهذا لأن ذلك بمنزلة أعراض تحدث، فلا يصلح أن يكون وجود شيء علةً لوجود غيره. ألا يُرى أن عدم الملك : لا يمنعُ الملك، وعدم الشراء: لا يمنع حدوث الشراء، ووجودَ الملك : لا يمنع الزوال، وهذا لا يُشكل، ألا يُرى أن النسخ في دلائل الشرع إنما صح لما ذكرنا.
As for arguing by istishab al-hal, it is valid according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, regarding every ruling that is established and its obligation known by its proof, then its cessation occurs; the istishab of the state of remaining upon that is a proof that validly argues against the opponent. According to us (Hanafis): This is not a proof for obligation, but it is a defensive proof. Their issues indicate that: We have said regarding sulh ala al-inkar: it is permissible. And we did not make the original innocence of the debt a proof against the claimant; rather, the claimant's statement becomes equal to his statement. And al-Shafi'i made it a proof (mujib) such that it extended to the claimant and invalidated his claim, and invalidated the sulh. And we said regarding a share (shiqs) when sold from a house, and the partner seeks pre-emption (shuf'a), and the seller denies the ownership of the claimant in what he possesses: the statement is his statement, so shuf'a is not obligatory except with evidence. And al-Shafi'i said: it is obligatory without evidence. Likewise, a man said to his slave: 'If you do not enter the house today, you are free.' Then the day passed, and it is not known whether he entered or not; then they differed. The statement is the master's statement according to us, for what we mentioned. And he (al-Shafi'i) argued that when a ruling is established by its proof, it remains by that same proof; do you not see that the ruling of the text remains with it after the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him, until abrogation becomes impossible. And he argued by their consensus that if one is certain of wudu, no other wudu is required of him, and he must perform prayer with what he knows even if he doubts the hadath. And if he knows the hadath, then doubts wudu, the hadath remains. And if the ownership of the pre-emptor is established by the buyer's admission that it was his, or that he bought it from so-and-so who owned it, then shuf'a becomes obligatory; and his ownership remains due to the absence of what removes it; and despite that, it has become a proof that obliges. And similarly, if the claimant's witnesses testified that this thing was his ownership, it becomes a proof that obliges. And we (Hanafis) argue that the proof that obliges a ruling does not necessitate its remaining, just as bringing into existence does not necessitate remaining, to the extent that annihilation is valid. This is because it is like accidents that occur; so existence of a thing cannot be the cause of existence of another thing. Do you not see that non-ownership does not prevent ownership, and non-purchase does not prevent the occurrence of purchase, and existence of ownership does not prevent its removal; this is not ambiguous. Do you not see that abrogation in the proofs of the Law is only valid due to what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity). The Shafi'i position holds that istishab is a proof that can establish an obligation; the Hanafi position holds that it is only a defensive proof, i.e., it can rebut a claim but cannot by itself impose a duty. The author provides several examples to illustrate the difference: (1) Sulh ala al-inkar (settlement based on denial): Hanafis allow it because the defendant's original innocence (bara'at al-dhimma) is not a proof against the claimant; it merely defends. Shafi'is invalidate the settlement because they treat the presumed innocence as a proof that defeats the claim. (2) Shuf'a (pre-emption): Hanafis require the claimant to provide evidence of ownership; Shafi'is consider the claimant's mere claim sufficient due to istishab of his ownership. (3) Conditional manumission: If a slave fails to enter by day's end and it is unknown, Hanafis say the master's word is taken (presumption of continued slavery); Shafi'is would presumably side with the slave. The Shafi'i argument is that a ruling once established remains by its proof, analogizing to the permanence of Quranic rulings after the Prophet's death and the example of certainty in wudu. The Hanafi rebuttal is that the proof that establishes a ruling does not necessarily cause its continuation, just as creation does not imply perpetuity; rulings can change, and abrogation is possible. The examples demonstrate that Hanafis do not treat istishab as a creative proof (hujja mujiba) but only as a defensive one (hujja dafi'a).

Key Terms
استصحاب الحال (istishāb al-ḥāl): Presumption of continuity; the principle that a state of affairs is presumed to continue until evidence of change arises.
حجة دافعة (ḥujja dāfi'a): A defensive proof that repels a claim but does not establish a positive ruling.
حجة موجبة (ḥujja mūjiba): A proof that establishes an obligation or new ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is sulh ala al-inkar (settlement based on denial) permissible?
A) Because the defendant's denial is proven by evidence.
B) Because the claimant's claim is weak.
C) Because the original innocence (bara'at al-dhimma) is only a defensive proof, not a proof against the claimant.
D) Because the settlement is based on mutual agreement.

Key Takeaway
Istishab al-hal is a defensive proof (hujja dafi'a) in the Hanafi school, preserving the status quo but not creating new obligations, a position that distinguishes it from the Shafi'i school.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Categories of Ikhtilaf Requiring Textual Evidence
Taxonomic categorization of ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the five types of ikhtilaf (mujib, sifat sabab, shart, hukm, sifat hukm) that cannot be resolved by qiyas, and explain why textual evidence is required for each.

Core Idea
Legal disagreements over cause, condition, or modality of a ruling demand nass, not analogy.

Al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh distinguishes between disagreements that can be settled by qiyas and those that must be resolved by direct textual evidence. This lesson maps the five categories he identifies, each with concrete examples from Hanafi law, and explains why personal reasoning (ra'y) is insufficient for such fundamental issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories
Read the passage carefully and list the five types of ikhtilaf: over the effective cause (mujib), description of cause (sifat al-sabab), condition (shart), ruling (hukm), and description of ruling (sifat al-hukm).
Each category is introduced with 'أما'.
2
Match examples to categories
For each category, note the examples given: riba and prayer for mujib; pasturing (sa'imah) and halal for sifat sabab; basmalah, i'tikaf, witnesses, marriage for shart; one rak'ah, fasting part-day, etc. for hukm; witr, udhiyah, 'umrah, mortgage, payment, sale for sifat hukm.
Some examples appear in multiple categories; check the Arabic text for precise placement.
3
Explain the reason qiyas is inapplicable
For each category, articulate why qiyas fails: the cause itself is disputed (mujib), the attribute of the cause is ambiguous (sifat sabab), the condition is purely textual (shart), the ruling's existence is contested (hukm), or its modality is debated (sifat hukm).
The author states explicitly that such disagreements 'cannot be established by opinion'.
4
Analyze the textual indication method
Study how the author uses nass (text) to resolve the riba and prayer examples: for riba, he invokes textual indication (dalala) regarding excess without countervalue; for prayer, he cites the hadith 'Accept Allah's charity' and reasoning about concession.
This illustrates the Hanafi approach of relying on implication (dalala) and requirement (iqtida') of texts.
5
Contrast with a potential qiyas-based argument
Consider how a scholar might erroneously apply qiyas to these cases (e.g., analogizing riba from other sales, or prayer shortening from travel analogies). Identify why the author rejects such reasoning.
The passage argues that in these categories, the basis of the ruling is not an 'illah that can be extended by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما تفسير القسم الأول : فمثل قولهم في الجنس بانفراده: إنه يُحرِّم النسيئة، فهذا خلافٌ وقع في الموجب للحكم، فلم يصح إثباته بالرأي، ولا نفيه به، إنما يجب الكلامُ فيه بإشارة النص، أو دلالته، أو اقتضائه. وكذلك اختلافُهم في السفر أنه مُسقِطٌ لشطر الصلاة، أم لا؟ لا يصح التكلم فيه بالقياس، بل بما ذكرنا. فقلنا في مسألة الجنس إنا وجدنا الفضل الذي لا يقابله عوض عقد المعاوضة محرَّماً بما ذكرنا من العلة، ووجدنا هذا حكماً تستوي شبهته بحقيقته حتى لا يجوز البيع مجازفة؛ لاحتمال الربا. وقد وجدنا في النسيئة شبهة الفضل، وهو الحلول المضاف إلى صنع العباد، وقد وجدنا شبهة العلة وهو أحد وصفي العلة، فأثبتناه بدلالة النص. وكذلك فعلنا في السفر؛ لأن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «إن الله تعالى تصدق عليكم، فاقبلوا صدقته». وذلك إسقاط محض، فلا يصح ردُّه، ولأن القصر تعيّن تخفيفاً، بخلاف الفطر في السفر. ولأن التخيير على وجه لا يتضمن رفقاً بالعبد ونفعاً : من صفات العبودية، على ما عُرف فهذه دلالات النصوص. (١) باب بيان حكم العلة حديث : إن الله تصدق عليكم: تقدم في باب وجوه الوقوف على أحكام النظم [ ص ٣٣٧] .

وأما صفة السبب : فمثل صفة السَّوْم في الأنعام، أيشترط للزكاة، ومثل صفة الحلّ في الوطء لإثبات حرمة المصاهرة. ومثل اختلافهم في صفة القتل الموجب للكفارة، وفي صفة اليمين الموجبة للكفارة.

وأما اختلافهم في الشرط : فمثل اختلافهم في شرط التسمية في الذبيحة، ومثل صوم الاعتكاف ومثل الشهود في النكاح، ومثل شرط النكاح لصحة الطلاق عند الشافعي رحمه الله. والاختلاف في صفته مثل صفة الشهود في النكاح رجال أم رجال ونساء؟ عدول لا محالة، أم شهود موصوفون بكل وصف، وكقولنا : إن الوضوء شرط بغير نية.

وأما الاختلاف في الحكم فمثل اختلافهم في الركعة الواحدة، وفي صوم بعض اليوم، وفي حَرَم المدينة، ومثل إشعار البدن.

وأما صفته : فمثل اختلافهم في صفة الوتر، وفي صفة الأضحية، وفي صفة العمرة وفي صفة حكم الرهن بعد اتفاقهم أنه وثيقة لجانب الاستيفاء، وكاختلافهم في كيفية وجوب المهر، وفي كيفية حكم البيع أنه ثابت بنفسه، أم متراخ إلى قطع المجلس ؟

ولا يلزم اختلافُ الناس بالرأي في صوم يوم النحر؛ لأنهم لم يختلفوا أن الصوم مشروع في الأيام، وإنما اختلفوا في صفة حكم النهي، وذلك لا يثبت بالرأي.
As for the interpretation of the first category: it is like their statement concerning the genus (jins) alone: that it prohibits nasi'ah (delayed payment). This is a disagreement that occurred concerning the effective cause (mujib) of the ruling. It is not valid to affirm it by personal opinion, nor to negate it by it. Rather, speech about it must be based on the indication of the text, its implication, or its requirement. Likewise their disagreement concerning travel: whether it causes the dropping of half of the prayer or not. It is not valid to speak about it by analogy (qiyas), but rather by what we have mentioned. So we said concerning the issue of genus: we found that the excess (fadl) that is not met by a countervalue in a commutative contract is prohibited due to the cause we mentioned, and we found this ruling to be such that its doubtful resemblance is equal to its reality, so that sale without specification is not permitted due to the possibility of riba. And we found in nasi'ah a similarity to excess, which is the deferment attributed to the action of people, and we found a similarity to the cause, which is one of the two attributes of the cause, so we established it by the indication of the text. And we did likewise concerning travel, because the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Indeed Allah has given you charity, so accept His charity.' That is a pure dropping, so it is not valid to reject it, and because the shortening is specifically a concession, unlike breaking the fast during travel. And because choice in a manner that does not include comfort for the servant and benefit is from the attributes of servitude, as is known; these are the indications of the texts. (1) Section on the explanation of the ruling of the cause: Hadith 'Indeed Allah has given you charity' preceded in the chapter on ways of pausing at the rulings of the arrangement [p. 337].
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is from Al-Bazdawi's usul work, categorizing types of ikhtilaf (disagreement) that cannot be resolved by personal reasoning (ra'y) or analogy (qiyas) but must be based on textual evidence (nass). The author first discusses the category of disagreement over the 'effective cause' (mujib al-hukm). The example given is the prohibition of riba (usury) in bartering the same genus (jins) with delay – some scholars disagreed whether the cause is the genus alone or presence of excess. Al-Bazdawi argues that such disagreements require textual indication, not analogy. The second example is whether travel causes the shortening of prayer; the Prophet's hadith about accepting Allah's charity is cited as textual proof. The passage then enumerates other categories: disagreement over the description of the cause (sifat al-sabab), such as whether the animal pastured freely (sa'imah) is a condition for zakat, or the description of 'hull' (being permissible) for establishing marital prohibition. Next is disagreement over the condition (shart), e.g., pronouncing basmalah in slaughter, fasting for i'tikaf, witnesses in marriage, and the Shafi'i condition of marriage for valid divorce. The author notes that the Hanafi position on wudu is that it is a condition without intention. Then comes disagreement over the ruling (hukm) itself, like one rak'ah constituting prayer, fasting part of a day, the sanctity of Madinah, and marking sacrificial animals. Finally, disagreement over the description of the ruling (sifat al-hukm), such as the nature of witr prayer, udhiyah, 'umrah, the legal effect of a mortgage, marriage dowry obligation, and contract formation. The passage concludes that disagreement about fasting on the Day of Nahr (sacrifice) does not stem from ra'y because all agree that fasting is generally legislated; the disagreement is over the prohibition's description, which cannot be established by opinion.

Key Terms
الموجب للحكم (al-mujib li al-hukm): The effective cause that necessitates a ruling
صفة السبب (sifat al-sabab): The description or attribute of the cause (sabab) that is subject to disagreement
الشرط (al-shart): A condition whose absence prevents the ruling but whose presence does not cause it
الحكم (al-hukm): The legal ruling itself, such as obligation or prohibition
صفة الحكم (sifat al-hukm): The description or modality of the ruling, e.g., whether it is immediate or deferred

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of disagreement over the effective cause (mujib) of a ruling?
A) Whether witnessing in marriage requires men or men and women
B) Whether the genus alone prohibits delayed payment (nasi'ah) in barter
C) Whether the basmalah is a condition for lawful slaughter
D) Whether one rak'ah constitutes a valid prayer

Key Takeaway
Legal disagreements over the cause, condition, or modality of a ruling cannot be settled by qiyas; only textual evidence (nass) suffices.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced

Classification of Ikhtilaf: Attributes of Cause, Condition, and Ruling
Classification of ikhtilaf and condition of valid qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify scholarly disagreements (ikhtilaf) into three types—regarding the attribute of the cause, condition, or ruling—and apply the Hanafi rule that qiyas requires an established origin in Shariah.

Core Idea
Not all ikhtilaf is valid; valid disagreement must be based on a Shariah origin, not pure opinion.

In this passage, Imam al-Bazdawi presents a systematic classification of ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh. He distinguishes disagreements about the attribute of the cause, the condition, and the ruling, providing concrete fiqh examples. He then introduces a critical principle: qiyas (analogy) is only valid when there exists an established origin (asl) in the Shariah. Without such an origin, reasoning by opinion is invalid. This lesson will help you navigate the boundaries of valid scholarly difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of ikhtilaf
The passage divides disagreements into those concerning the attribute of the cause (sifat al-sabab), the attribute of the condition (sifat al-shart), and the attribute of the ruling (sifat al-hukm). List them with examples from the text.
Example for cause: whether being offered for sale is a condition for zakat on livestock.
2
Distinguish the attribute of the cause from the condition
Understand the conceptual difference: cause (sabab) triggers the ruling, while condition (shart) is an external requirement. For instance, the attribute of being offered for sale vs. saying Bismillah in slaughter.
The Hanafi view treats conditions as separate from causes.
3
Analyze an example of ikhtilaf in the ruling
Disagreement about the ruling itself, such as whether one rak'ah of witr is obligatory or whether fasting part of a day is valid.
This concerns the hukm level (wajib vs. sunnah).
4
Examine the validity of qiyas
The passage argues that qiyas is only valid if there is an established origin (asl) in Shariah. Examples: taqabud analogy to sarf (valid) vs. tasmiyah in slaughter (invalid due to lack of origin).
Qiyas without an origin is pure opinion (ra'y) and not binding.
5
Apply the principle to distinguish valid from invalid ikhtilaf
Given a scenario, determine whether the ikhtilaf is based on a Shariah origin or pure opinion. Use the day of Nahr example as a test.
The text says the disagreement about the prohibition's attribute cannot be settled by opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صفة السبب : فمثل صفة السَّوْم في الأنعام، أيشترط للزكاة، ومثل صفة الحلّ في الوطء لإثبات حرمة المصاهرة. ومثل اختلافهم في صفة القتل الموجب للكفارة، وفي صفة اليمين الموجبة للكفارة. وأما اختلافهم في الشرط : فمثل اختلافهم في شرط التسمية في الذبيحة، ومثل صوم الاعتكاف ومثل الشهود في النكاح، ومثل شرط النكاح لصحة الطلاق عند الشافعي رحمه الله. والاختلاف في صفته مثل صفة الشهود في النكاح رجال أم رجال ونساء؟ عدول لا محالة، أم شهود موصوفون بكل وصف، :وكقولنا : إن الوضوء شرط بغير نية . وأما الاختلاف في الحكم فمثل اختلافهم في الركعة الواحدة، وفي صوم بعض اليوم، وفي حَرَم المدينة، ومثل إشعار البدن. وأما صفته : فمثل اختلافهم في صفة الوتر، وفي صفة الأضحية، وفي صفة العمرة وفي صفة حكم الرهن بعد اتفاقهم أنه وثيقة لجانب الاستيفاء، وكاختلافهم في كيفية وجوب المهر، وفي كيفية حكم البيع أنه ثابت بنفسه، أم متراخ إلى قطع المجلس ؟ ولا يلزم اختلافُ الناس بالرأي في صوم يوم النحر ؛ لأنهم لم يختلفوا أن الصوم مشروع في الأيام، وإنما اختلفوا في صفة حكم النهي، وذلك لا يثبت بالرأي. لا يصح تعليله : وإنما أنكرنا هذه الجملة إذا لم يوجد في الشريعة أصل فأما إذا وجد : فلا بأس به، ألا يُرى أنهم اختلفوا في التقابض في بيع الطعام بالطعام وتكلموا فيه بالرأي ؛ لأنا وجدنا لإثباته أصلاً، وهو الصرف، ووجدنا لجوازه بدونه أصلاً، وهو بيع سائر السلع، فإذا وجد مثله في غيره : صحت له التعدية. و ألا يُرى أن مَن ادعى إيجاب التسمية في الذبيحة شرطاً بالقياس : لم يجد له أصلاً، ومن أراد إيجاب الصوم في الاعتكاف شرطاً بالقياس : لم يجد له أصلاً أيضاً. وهذا باب لا يُحصى عدد ،فروعه فاقتصرنا فيه على الإشارة إلى الجُمَل.
As for the attribute of the cause: such as the attribute of being offered for sale (al-sawm) in livestock, whether it is a condition for zakat; and such as the attribute of being permissible (al-hill) in intercourse to establish prohibition by affinity. And such as their disagreement regarding the attribute of killing that necessitates expiation, and regarding the attribute of an oath that necessitates expiation. As for their disagreement in the condition: such as their disagreement about the condition of saying 'Bismillah' in slaughter, and such as the fast of i'tikaf, and such as witnesses in marriage, and such as the condition of marriage for the validity of divorce according to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him). And disagreement regarding its attribute: such as the attribute of witnesses in marriage: are they men, or men and women? Must they be upright ('udul) without exception, or witnesses described with every description? And as our statement: ablution is a condition without intention. As for disagreement in the ruling: such as their disagreement about one rak'ah, and about fasting part of a day, and about the sanctuary of Madinah, and such as marking the sacrificial animal. As for its attribute: such as their disagreement about the attribute of witr, and about the attribute of sacrifice, and about the attribute of 'umrah, and about the attribute of the ruling of pledge after their agreement that it is a security for the side of collection, and as their disagreement about the modality of obligation of the dower, and about the modality of the ruling of sale: is it established by itself, or deferred to the termination of the session? And the disagreement of people by opinion in fasting the day of Nahr is not binding; because they did not disagree that fasting is legislated in days, but they disagreed regarding the attribute of the ruling of prohibition, and that is not established by opinion. Its reasoning is not valid. And we only reject this entirety when there is no origin in Shariah. But if there is an origin, then there is no problem. Do they not see that they disagreed about immediate taking (taqabud) in sale of food for food and spoke about it by opinion? Because we found an origin for its establishment, which is sarf (currency exchange), and we found an origin for its permissibility without it, which is sale of other commodities. So if a similar case is found in another, then extending the ruling is valid. And do they not see that whoever claimed the obligation of saying 'Bismillah' in slaughter as a condition by analogy did not find an origin for it, and whoever wanted to obligate fasting in i'tikaf as a condition by analogy also did not find an origin. And this is a chapter whose branches cannot be counted, so we suffice with pointing to the general principles.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage is a classification of types of scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) in usul al-fiqh by Imam al-Bazdawi. He categorizes differences into three aspects: the attribute of the cause (sabab), the attribute of the condition (shart), and the attribute of the ruling (hukm). For each, he provides fiqh examples. For instance, regarding the cause: scholars disagree whether being offered for sale is a condition for zakat on livestock, and whether the attribute of being permissible (hill) in intercourse establishes the prohibition of affinity. Regarding the condition: examples include whether saying 'Bismillah' is a condition for slaughter, the fast of i'tikaf, and the requirement of witnesses in marriage. He also notes that some, like Imam al-Shafi'i, consider marriage a condition for divorce. Regarding the ruling: scholars differ on whether one rak'ah (of witr) is obligatory, fasting part of a day, and the status of the sanctuary of Madinah. The text then asserts that disagreement based purely on opinion (ra'y) is not valid unless there is an origin in Shariah. For example, the disagreement about fasting on the day of Nahr (10th Dhul Hijjah) is not about whether fasting is legislated, but about the nature of the prohibition—and that cannot be settled by opinion. The author explains that analogy (qiyas) is only valid when there is an established origin. For instance, the ruling on immediate taking in food sales is based on the analogy to currency exchange (sarf) or general sales. But if no origin exists, like obligating the tasmiyah in slaughter by qiyas, it is invalid. The passage concludes that this chapter has many branches, and the author only points to general principles.

Key Terms
صفة السبب (sifat al-sabab): The attribute or description of the legal cause (sabab) that triggers a ruling.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that must be present for a ruling or act to be valid.
الحكم (al-hukm): The legal ruling itself, such as obligation, prohibition, permissibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is disagreement about fasting on the day of Nahr not binding according to this passage?
A) Because fasting on that day is unanimously prohibited.
B) Because scholars agreed that fasting is legislated; the disagreement is about the attribute of the prohibition, which cannot be settled by opinion.
C) Because opinion is never valid in usul.
D) Because the day of Nahr is like other days.

Key Takeaway
Valid scholarly disagreement must be based on a Shariah origin; qiyas without an asl is invalid in Hanafi usul.

Dispute on Taswib al-Mujtahidin: Unity of Truth vs. Multiple Correct Interpretations
Presents arguments for and against unity of truth in ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key arguments in the dispute on whether every mujtahid is correct and explain the Hanafi position that truth is one while the mujtahid who errs is still rewarded.

Core Idea
Truth is one with Allah; a mujtahid may be correct or mistaken, but all are rewarded for sincere effort.

This lesson examines a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: does every mujtahid hit the divine truth (haqq), or is truth singular? The passage reports Abu Hanifa's nuanced view—every mujtahid is 'correct' in the sense of being excused, but the actual truth is one. We will analyze the arguments for multiple correct outcomes (using the qibla analogy and prophetic diversity) and the Hanafi response grounded in explicit scriptural proofs from the Quran and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
State the core positions
Identify the two main views: (1) Every mujtahid is correct (musib) in that multiple contradictory rulings can all be true haqq; (2) Truth is singular with Allah, and only one mujtahid hits it, though others are rewarded for their effort.
The Hanafi position is a middle ground: taswib in terms of reward, takhti' (error) in terms of the actual haqq.
2
Analyze the argument for multiple truths
Examine the qibla analogy: just as mujtahids in determining qibla are all considered correct in fulfilling the prayer, so too in legal rulings. Also consider the argument from prophetic diversity: different prophets had different laws, and laws change over time.
These analogies suggest that haqq can be relative to the mujtahid's evidence or context.
3
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
Understand how the Hanafi response distinguishes between acceptance of the act (the prayer is valid due to valid ijtihad) and the actual truth of the direction. Similarly, in rulings, the effort is rewarded, but the truth is singular.
The qibla analogy does not prove multiplicity of haqq; it only proves validity of the act.
4
Evaluate the scriptural proofs
Analyze Quran 21:79, where Solomon is singled out for understanding, implying the other prophet erred. Also consider the hadith of Amr ibn al-As: if correct ten rewards, if wrong one reward. These show that error is possible and recognized, yet the mistaken is rewarded.
These texts establish that truth is one (Solomon's understanding) and that error (the other's) is possible and excused.
5
Conclude with the tarjih
The weight of evidence supports the Hanafi position: truth is one, but a mistaken mujtahid is not sinful; he is rewarded for his ijtihad. This balances divine objectivity with human fallibility.
This is the relied-upon view in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد روي ذلك عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه قال : كل مجتهد مصيب، والحق عند الله تعالى واحد. ومعنى هذا الكلام ما قلنا احتج من ادعى الحقوق: بأن المجتهدين جميعاً لما كلّفوا إصابة الحق، وجب القول ولا يتحقق ذلك على ما في وسعهم إلا أن يُجعل الحقُّ متعدداً: بتعدده؛ تحقيقاً لشرط التكليف، كما قيل في المجتهدين في القبلة إنهم جعلوا مصيبين، حتى تأدى الفرضُ عنهم جميعاً ، ولا يتأدى الفرض عنهم إلا بإصابة المأمور به مع إحاطة العلم بخطأ من استدبر الكعبة. وجائز تعدد الحقوق في الحظر والإباحة عند قيام الدليل كما صح ذلك عند اختلاف الرسل وعلى اختلاف الزمان فكذلك عند اختلاف المكلفين. ومن قال باستواء الحقوق قال : لأن دليل التعدد لم يوجب التفاوت. ووجه القول الآخر : أن استواءها يقطع التكليف؛ لأنها إذا استوت: أصيبت بمجرد الاختيار من غير امتحان وسقطت درجة العلماء، وبطلت الدعوة، وسقطت وجوه النظر. ألا يُرى أن الاختلاف في اختيار وجوه كفارة اليمين باطل، وأن اختياره بمجرد العزيمة صحيح بلا تأمل، فلذلك وجب القول بأن بعضها أحق. ووجه قولنا إن الحق واحد وأن المجتهد يصيب مرة، ويخطىء أخرى قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَفَهَمْنَهَا سُلَيْمَنَ وَكُلًّا وَالَيْنَا حُكْمًا وَعِلْمًا﴾ الأنبياء /79 وإذا اختص سليمان صلوات الله وسلامه عليه بالفهم، وهو إصابة الحق بالنظر فيه : كان الآخر خطاً. وقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لعمرو بن العاص رضي الله عنه: احكم على أنك إن أصبت فلك عشر حسنات، وإن أخطأت فلك حسنة. وقال ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه في حديث المفوضة: إن أصبت فمن الله ، وإن أخطأت فمن ابن أم عبد - وفي رواية: فمني ومن الشيطان، والله ورسوله منه بريئان
It has been narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that he said: Every mujtahid is correct, and the truth with Allah is one. The meaning of this statement is what we have said. Those who claim multiple correct opinions argued: Since all mujtahids are obligated to hit the truth, it is necessary to assert that this obligation is fulfilled only if truth is multiplied according to their multiplicity, as was said regarding mujtahids in the qibla—they are deemed correct, so the obligation is performed by all of them, but the obligation would not be performed by them except by hitting the commanded act, despite the knowledge that one who turned his back to the Kaaba is mistaken. It is permissible for truths to be multiple in prohibition and permission when evidence exists, just as it is established in the differences of messengers and in different times, so similarly in the differences of those obliged. One who argued for equality of truths said: because the evidence of multiplicity did not necessitate difference. The other argument: their equality cuts off the obligation; because if they are equal, they are attained by mere choice without examination, and the rank of scholars falls, the call (to truth) becomes invalid, and aspects of consideration fall. Do you not see that disagreement in choosing types of expiation for an oath is invalid, and that choosing it by mere resolve is valid without reflection? Therefore, it is necessary to say that some are more deserving. The proof for our statement that truth is one and that the mujtahid sometimes hits and sometimes misses: Allah said, "So We gave understanding to Solomon, and to each We gave wisdom and knowledge" (Quran 21:79). Since Solomon was singled out for understanding—which is hitting the truth through consideration—the other was in error. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Amr ibn al-As (may Allah be pleased with him): "Judge, and if you are correct, you have ten rewards; if you err, you have one reward." Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said regarding the hadith of al-Mufawwadah: "If I am correct, it is from Allah; if I err, it is from Ibn Umm Abd—and in one narration: from me and from Satan, and Allah and His Messenger are innocent of it."
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage addresses the classic usul al-fiqh dispute about taswib al-mujtahidin—whether every mujtahid is correct (musib) or only one is correct and others are mistaken (mukhti’). It opens by reporting Abu Hanifa’s statement that every mujtahid is correct, but immediately clarifies that truth (al-haqq) is one with Allah, implying that while all are excused and rewarded, only the one who actually hits the truth is truly correct. The text then presents the argument of those who claim multiple correct opinions (al-mu'adhdhirun or al-mujawwizun), using the analogy of ijtihad regarding the qibla: all who pray toward a direction they believe is correct are deemed to have fulfilled the obligation, even if some are actually facing away from the Kaaba. They extend this to rulings: just as different prophets brought different laws, and laws changed over time, so too can different mujtahids have different correct rulings in their own contexts. The counterargument is then given: if all opinions were equally correct, the very purpose of ijtihad as a test would collapse—there would be no distinction between a scholar and a layman, and ijtihad would become mere choice without intellectual effort. The author then presents the Hanafi position supporting the unity of truth, citing the Quranic verse (21:79) where Allah says He gave understanding (fahm) specifically to Solomon, implying that the other prophet (David) did not hit the correct understanding in that particular case—thus truth is one and the other was wrong. The hadith of the Prophet to Amr ibn al-As reinforces this: if the judge is correct, he gets ten rewards; if wrong, one reward. This shows that error is possible and recognized, yet the mistaken mujtahid is still rewarded, not sinful. Ibn Mas'ud's statement further illustrates that when correct, it is from Allah; when mistaken, it is from human fallibility and Satan. Thus, the Hanafi school teaches that truth is one, but a mujtahid who errs is not sinful—rather, he is rewarded for his effort. This is the doctrine of takhti’ (declaring error of some) combined with taswib in the sense of exoneration.

Key Terms
مجتهد (mujtahid): A qualified jurist who exerts maximum effort to derive a ruling from the divine sources.
مصيب (musib): One who is correct in hitting the actual divine ruling (al-haqq).
حق (haqq): The actual divine ruling that Allah intends for the specific case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary textual proof for the Hanafi position that truth is one?
A) The hadith of Amr ibn al-As: 'If correct, ten rewards; if wrong, one reward.'
B) The Quranic verse: 'So We gave understanding to Solomon, and to each We gave wisdom and knowledge.'
C) The statement of Ibn Mas'ud: 'If correct, from Allah; if wrong, from me and Satan.'
D) The agreement of the Sahaba on the qibla analogy.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, truth (al-haqq) is one with Allah; a mujtahid who errs is rewarded for his effort, but only the one who hits the truth is truly correct.

Ijtihad Can Err: Proof from Texts
Proving ijtihad can err using prophetic traditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the textual proofs that ijtihad can err and explain the Hanafi position against the Mu'tazili taswib view.

Core Idea
Ijtihad can err; the mujtahid is rewarded for effort, but only one ruling is correct.

This passage from al-Bazdawi tackles a foundational usul question: can a mujtahid be wrong? The author presents several hadith proofs to show that error is possible, contrary to the Mu'tazili view that every mujtahid is correct. Understanding this sets the stage for the second part about the impossibility of multiple correct rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its opponents
Recognize that the author is asserting ijtihad can err, opposing the Mu'tazili view of taswib (every mujtahid is correct).
2
Examine each textual evidence
List the four proofs: (1) Solomon vs. other judge, (2) hadith of Amr ibn al-As, (3) hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, (4) besieging hadith. For each, understand how it demonstrates the possibility of error.
Focus on the explicit language of reward for error in the Amr hadith.
3
Understand the import of reward for error
The hadith of Amr shows that even erroneous ijtihad earns reward, proving that error is recognized by the Lawgiver.
This refutes the rational argument that duty implies correctness.
4
Recognize the Hanafi response to Mu'tazila
The Hanafi position is that obligation to strive does not guarantee hitting the truth; error is possible and rewarded as an act of obedience.
The thamarah al-khilaf: later mujtahids can overrule previous erroneous rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا اختص سليمان صلوات الله وسلامه عليه بالفهم، وهو إصابة الحق بالنظر فيه : كان الآخر خطأ. وقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لعمرو بن العاص رضي الله عنه: احكم على أنك إن أصبت فلك عشر حسنات، وإن أخطأت فلك حسنة (1). وقال ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه في حديث المفوضة: إن أصبت فمن الله ، وإن أخطأت فمن ابن أم عبد - وفي رواية: فمني ومن الشيطان، والله ورسوله منه بريئان (۲). وقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : (وإذا حاصرتم حصناً فأرادوكم أن تنزلوهم على حكم الله : فلا تُنزلوهم على حكم الله، فإنكم لا تدرون ما حكم الله فيهم (١). وهذا دليل على احتمال الخطأ. ولأن تعدد الحقوق ممتنع استدلالاً بنفس الحكم، وسببه. أما السبب فلأنا قلنا إن القياس تعديةٌ وُضع لدرك الحكم، فما ليس بمتعدد : لا يتعدى متعدداً؛ لأنه يصير تغييراً حينئذ، فيوجب ذلك أن يكون الحق متعدداً بالنص بعينه، وهذا خلاف الإجماع. ألا يُرى أنه لو توهمناه غير معلول : لم يكن حكمه متعدداً، وذلك مما لا تحتمله صيغته بيقين فلا يتعدد بالتعليل وفيه تغييره، ويصير الفرع به مخالفاً للأصل. وأما الاستدلال بنفس الحكم فهو أن الفطر والصوم، وفساد الصلاة وصحتها، وفساد النكاح وصحته، ووجودَ الشيء وعدمه، وقيام الحظر والإباحة في شيء واحدٍ يستحيل اجتماعه ولا يصلح المستحيل حكماً شرعياً، وصحة التكليف تحصل بما قلنا من صحة الاجتهاد وإصابته ابتداء. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في مدعي الميراث إذا لم يشهد شهوده: إنا لا نعلم له وارثاً غيره : إني لا أكفّل المدعي.
And when Solomon (peace be upon him) was singled out for understanding, which is hitting the truth by reflection in it, the other was mistaken. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Amr ibn al-As (may God be pleased with him): 'Judge, and if you are correct you have ten good deeds, and if you are mistaken you have one good deed.' And Ibn Mas'ud (may God be pleased with him) said in the hadith of the woman with a disputed dowry: 'If I am correct, it is from God; if I am mistaken, it is from Ibn Umm Abd (himself) - and in another narration: from me and from Satan, and God and His Messenger are innocent of it.' And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When you besiege a fort and they ask you to make them descend according to the judgment of God, do not make them descend according to the judgment of God, for you do not know what God's judgment concerning them is.' This is evidence for the possibility of error. And because multiplicity of rights is impossible, deduced from the ruling itself and from its cause. As for the cause, we said that qiyas is an extension placed to attain rulings; what is not multiple does not extend to multiple, because then it becomes a change, and that necessarily would make the right multiple by the very text, which is contrary to consensus. Do you not see that if we assumed it to be without cause, its ruling would not be multiple, and its wording does not admit that with certainty, so it does not become multiple by ta'lil and there is change in it, and the branch becomes contrary to the original. And as for deduction from the ruling itself, it is that breaking fast and fasting, invalidity of prayer and its validity, invalidity of marriage and its validity, existence of something and its non-existence, and prohibition and permissibility in one thing are impossible to combine, and the impossible cannot be a valid legal ruling, and the validity of obligation is attained by what we said about the validity of ijtihad and its hitting the truth initially. And Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him) said regarding a claimant of inheritance when his witnesses do not testify: 'We do not know any heir other than him: I will not make the claimant liable.'
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage establishes two key usul points. First, that ijtihad can result in error, contrary to the Mu'tazili position that every mujtahid is correct. The author cites three proofs: (1) The case of Solomon and the other judge—Solomon was given correct understanding, implying the other was wrong. (2) The hadith of Amr ibn al-As promising ten rewards for correctness and one for error, showing error is possible. (3) The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud about the case of a woman with a dowry dispute, where he says if he errs it is from himself and Satan. (4) The prophetic command not to make a besieged people descend according to God's judgment because you do not know what that judgment is, proving that human judgment may miss God's actual ruling. After establishing the possibility of error, the author argues that multiple correct rulings (ta'addud al-huquq) on a single matter are impossible. The reasoning is two-fold: from the cause (sabab) and from the ruling itself (hukm). From the cause: qiyas is an extension designed to derive the ruling; a non-multiple cause cannot yield multiple effects without changing the original text, which violates consensus. From the ruling itself: contradictory pairs like fasting and breaking fast, validity and invalidity, cannot coexist in one object; the impossible cannot be a legal ruling. Finally, Abu Hanifa's example illustrates that in inheritance cases, if the claimant presents witnesses who are not accepted, the judge does not hold the claimant liable to pay the inheritance, because the correct ruling is uncertain.

Key Terms
خطأ (khat'a): Error in ijtihad: the ruling that does not correspond to the actual divine ruling (hukm Allah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof that a mujtahid can err according to this passage?
A) The hadith of Amr ibn al-As showing reward for error.
B) The impossibility of multiple correct rulings.
C) The statement of Abu Hanifa about inheritance.
D) The consensus of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad can result in error, but the mujtahid is still rewarded for his sincere effort; only one ruling is correct.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

10 min
Why Multiple Correct Rulings Are Impossible
Arguing against the possibility of multiple correct rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two arguments used by Hanafi usulis to prove that multiple correct rulings (ta'addud al-huquq) are impossible and apply them to a simple case.

Core Idea
Multiple correct rulings on one case are impossible because they would combine contradictories, and qiyas cannot produce multiplicity.

Having established that ijtihad can err, the passage now argues that there cannot be multiple distinct correct rulings on the same matter. The author presents two lines of reasoning: one based on the nature of qiyas (the cause) and one based on the ruling itself. This is crucial for understanding how Hanafis view the binding nature of ijtihad and the role of consensus.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim: multiplicity is impossible
The author asserts that ta'addud al-huquq (multiple correct rulings) is impossible. This is a direct consequence of the previous point that ijtihad can err; if multiple rulings were correct, error would not exist.
This opposes views that accept taswib (every mujtahid correct).
2
First argument: from the cause (sabab)
The author argues that qiyas is an extension (ta'diyah) placed to attain rulings. A cause that is not multiple cannot yield multiple rulings without changing the text, which violates consensus.
Think of a single 'illah: it can only produce one hukm.
3
Second argument: from the ruling itself (hukm)
Contradictory rulings (e.g., valid/invalid, haram/halal) cannot coexist in one object. The impossible cannot be a valid ruling, so there can only be one correct ruling.
Pairs like fasting and breaking fast are mutually exclusive.
4
Apply Abu Hanifa's example
Abu Hanifa's example about inheritance demonstrates the practical stance: when the correct ruling is uncertain, the judge does not force a decision because only one ruling is correct and the judge may err.
This illustrates the reluctance to impose a ruling when the truth is not clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا اختص سليمان صلوات الله وسلامه عليه بالفهم، وهو إصابة الحق بالنظر فيه : كان الآخر خطأ. وقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لعمرو بن العاص رضي الله عنه: احكم على أنك إن أصبت فلك عشر حسنات، وإن أخطأت فلك حسنة (1). وقال ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه في حديث المفوضة: إن أصبت فمن الله ، وإن أخطأت فمن ابن أم عبد - وفي رواية: فمني ومن الشيطان، والله ورسوله منه بريئان (۲). وقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : (وإذا حاصرتم حصناً فأرادوكم أن تنزلوهم على حكم الله : فلا تُنزلوهم على حكم الله، فإنكم لا تدرون ما حكم الله فيهم (١). وهذا دليل على احتمال الخطأ. ولأن تعدد الحقوق ممتنع استدلالاً بنفس الحكم، وسببه. أما السبب فلأنا قلنا إن القياس تعديةٌ وُضع لدرك الحكم، فما ليس بمتعدد : لا يتعدى متعدداً؛ لأنه يصير تغييراً حينئذ، فيوجب ذلك أن يكون الحق متعدداً بالنص بعينه، وهذا خلاف الإجماع. ألا يُرى أنه لو توهمناه غير معلول : لم يكن حكمه متعدداً، وذلك مما لا تحتمله صيغته بيقين فلا يتعدد بالتعليل وفيه تغييره، ويصير الفرع به مخالفاً للأصل. وأما الاستدلال بنفس الحكم فهو أن الفطر والصوم، وفساد الصلاة وصحتها، وفساد النكاح وصحته، ووجودَ الشيء وعدمه، وقيام الحظر والإباحة في شيء واحدٍ يستحيل اجتماعه ولا يصلح المستحيل حكماً شرعياً، وصحة التكليف تحصل بما قلنا من صحة الاجتهاد وإصابته ابتداء. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في مدعي الميراث إذا لم يشهد شهوده: إنا لا نعلم له وارثاً غيره : إني لا أكفّل المدعي.
And when Solomon (peace be upon him) was singled out for understanding, which is hitting the truth by reflection in it, the other was mistaken. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Amr ibn al-As (may God be pleased with him): 'Judge, and if you are correct you have ten good deeds, and if you are mistaken you have one good deed.' And Ibn Mas'ud (may God be pleased with him) said in the hadith of the woman with a disputed dowry: 'If I am correct, it is from God; if I am mistaken, it is from Ibn Umm Abd (himself) - and in another narration: from me and from Satan, and God and His Messenger are innocent of it.' And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When you besiege a fort and they ask you to make them descend according to the judgment of God, do not make them descend according to the judgment of God, for you do not know what God's judgment concerning them is.' This is evidence for the possibility of error. And because multiplicity of rights is impossible, deduced from the ruling itself and from its cause. As for the cause, we said that qiyas is an extension placed to attain rulings; what is not multiple does not extend to multiple, because then it becomes a change, and that necessarily would make the right multiple by the very text, which is contrary to consensus. Do you not see that if we assumed it to be without cause, its ruling would not be multiple, and its wording does not admit that with certainty, so it does not become multiple by ta'lil and there is change in it, and the branch becomes contrary to the original. And as for deduction from the ruling itself, it is that breaking fast and fasting, invalidity of prayer and its validity, invalidity of marriage and its validity, existence of something and its non-existence, and prohibition and permissibility in one thing are impossible to combine, and the impossible cannot be a valid legal ruling, and the validity of obligation is attained by what we said about the validity of ijtihad and its hitting the truth initially. And Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him) said regarding a claimant of inheritance when his witnesses do not testify: 'We do not know any heir other than him: I will not make the claimant liable.'
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage establishes two key usul points. First, that ijtihad can result in error, contrary to the Mu'tazili position that every mujtahid is correct. The author cites three proofs: (1) The case of Solomon and the other judge—Solomon was given correct understanding, implying the other was wrong. (2) The hadith of Amr ibn al-As promising ten rewards for correctness and one for error, showing error is possible. (3) The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud about the case of a woman with a dowry dispute, where he says if he errs it is from himself and Satan. (4) The prophetic command not to make a besieged people descend according to God's judgment because you do not know what that judgment is, proving that human judgment may miss God's actual ruling. After establishing the possibility of error, the author argues that multiple correct rulings (ta'addud al-huquq) on a single matter are impossible. The reasoning is two-fold: from the cause (sabab) and from the ruling itself (hukm). From the cause: qiyas is an extension designed to derive the ruling; a non-multiple cause cannot yield multiple effects without changing the original text, which violates consensus. From the ruling itself: contradictory pairs like fasting and breaking fast, validity and invalidity, cannot coexist in one object; the impossible cannot be a legal ruling. Finally, Abu Hanifa's example illustrates that in inheritance cases, if the claimant presents witnesses who are not accepted, the judge does not hold the claimant liable to pay the inheritance, because the correct ruling is uncertain.

Key Terms
تعدد الحقوق (ta'addud al-huquq): The theory that multiple distinct correct rulings could apply to a single case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for a single case to have two correct rulings?
A) Because qiyas is an extension, and a non-multiple cause cannot yield multiple rulings.
B) Because the Prophet prohibited it.
C) Because Abu Hanifa said so.
D) Because the hadith of Amr says so.

Key Takeaway
Multiple correct rulings on one case are logically impossible because they lead to contradictions, and the nature of qiyas precludes multiplicity.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
9 min
Mujtahid Error and Correctness: The Qibla Case
Debate on whether mujtahid can be mistaken, using qibla example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a mujtahid can be factually mistaken yet his action is valid, using the qibla example to distinguish between error in objective and correctness in duty.

Core Idea
The mujtahid who errs in the objective is still correct in fulfilling his obligation.

This passage addresses a foundational usul debate: is a mujtahid always correct (musawwib) or can he be mistaken (mukhti')? The Hanafi school firmly holds that error is possible. The qibla example serves as a concrete test case: a person who performs ijtihad for the qibla and faces a wrong direction has indeed erred, yet his prayer is valid because he fulfilled his obligation of seeking (taharrin). This key distinction—between factual accuracy and duty fulfillment—anchors the Hanafi position. Let us work through the argument and its proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The core issue: after ijtihad, is the mujtahid’s conclusion always correct (every mujtahid is musib) or can it be mistaken (mukhti')? Note the Shafi'i position holds that the mujtahid must hit the actual object (e.g., physical Kaaba) to be correct.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (mukhtalaf fih).
2
Examine the Hanafi qibla argument
The text uses the qibla scenario: if a congregation prays after individual ijtihad, and someone knows the imam’s qibla is different, his prayer is invalid because he believes the imam is mistaken. If all directions were equally correct, such invalidity would not occur. This proves that error is recognized.
This is a reductio ad absurdum against the musawwiba (those who say every mujtahid is correct).
3
Analyze the justification for not repeating prayer
The one who errs in qibla does not repeat his prayer because he was only required to seek (taharrin), not to hit the exact Kaaba. The true objective is seeking Allah’s face; once that intention is fulfilled, the prayer is valid. The action (salah) is judged based on the mujtahid’s duty, not the objective.
Key distinction: khatta’ fi al-matlub (error in the sought object) vs. sawab fi nafs al-‘amal (correctness in the action itself).
4
Evaluate the proofs presented
The Hanafi side cites the hadith of Amr ibn al-As and Quran 21:79 (‘We gave judgment and knowledge’ – intended for action, not necessarily to hit the objective). The Shafi'i side cites the captives of Badr hadith and general use of ‘error’. Both are dalil naqli.
The Hanafi interpretation distinguishes between judgment (hukm) for action and hitting the objective.
5
Synthesize the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafi does not require repetition of prayers for a mistaken ijtihad (qibla or rulings); Shafi'i requires repetition if the actual object was missed. Also, in ijtihad, the Hanafi view allows for a range of valid opinions, while the musawwiba view collapses them into one truth.
This affects fatwa and adab al-mufti: multiple opinions can coexist as valid exertions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأما قوله : إن المخطئ للقبلة لا يعيد صلاته : فلأنه لا يُكلَّف إصابة الكعبة يقيناً ، بل كُلَّف طلبها على رجاء الإصابة، لكن الكعبة غير مقصودة ،بعينها، وإنما المقصود وجه الله تعالى واستقبال القبلة ابتلاء، فإذا حصل الابتلاء بما في قلبه من رجاء الإصابة، وحصل المقصود، وهو طلب وجه الله تعالى : سقطت حقيقته.
ألا يُرى أن جواز الصلاة وفسادها من صفات العمل، والمخطى في حق نفس العمل مصيب، فثبت أن مسألة القبلة ومسألتنا سواء، وهذا عندنا.
وعند الشافعي رحمه الله : كلف المتحري إصابة حقيقة الكعبة، حتى إذا أخطأ: أعاد صلواته.

And this is something the judges exercised caution in, and it is injustice (joor) named joor, and it is ijtihad; because it is, with respect to the one sought, deviating from the truth, and that is the meaning of injustice and oppression. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding the two li'ans (mutala'inayn) three by three: if the judge separates them, the ruling is executed and he has erred from the Sunnah. * And the evidence of what we said of the madhhab for our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) that the mujtahid errs and is correct in the books of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) is more than can be counted. As for the issue of the qibla: the madhhab in our view is that the one who exercises taharrin (seeking) also errs like other mujtahids. Do you not see that it was said in the Book of Prayer: if they prayed in congregation and sought the qibla and differed, then whoever among them knows the state of his imam to be in opposition, his prayer is invalid; because he is mistaken regarding the qibla according to him. And if all were correct and all directions were qibla, then his prayer would not be invalid, and they would not have been required to seek and search, like the congregation if they prayed inside the Kaaba. And as for his saying: the one mistaken regarding the qibla does not repeat his prayer: because he is not required to hit the Kaaba with certainty, but rather he is required to seek it with hope of hitting it. But the Kaaba is not intended in itself; rather what is intended is the face of Allah Almighty, and facing the qibla is a test. So when the test is achieved by what is in his heart of hope of hitting it, and the intended purpose is achieved, which is seeking the face of Allah Almighty, its reality falls away. Do you not see that the permissibility of prayer and its invalidity are attributes of the action, and the one who errs with respect to the action itself is correct. So it is established that the issue of the qibla and our issue are the same. And this is according to us. And according to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him): he required the one seeking to hit the reality of the Kaaba, such that if he errs, he repeats his prayers. As for the one who considers him mistaken from beginning to end: he argued with what we narrated from the general application of error in the hadith, and with the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the captives of Badr when the verse descended: 'If a book from Allah had preceded, it would have touched you...' (Surah al-Anfal 68). A punishment descended upon us; none survived except Umar. Our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) argued with the hadith of Amr ibn al-As (may Allah be pleased with him) and with the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And each one We gave judgment and knowledge' (Surah al-Anbiya 79). Judgment and knowledge are only intended for action. As for hitting the sought object, it comes from one of them. And Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said to Masruq and al-Aswad: 'Both of you were correct, and the action of Masruq is more beloved to me regarding what you preceded of the two rak'ahs of Maghrib.'
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage is a **usul dispute** presenting the Hanafi position that a mujtahid can be mistaken, using the qibla issue as a prime example. The text begins by noting a judicial practice of caution that is called 'injustice' but is actually ijtihad, and references a ruling by Imam Muhammad about li'an cases where the judge, though executing a valid ruling, errs from the Sunnah. The central argument is that mujtahids can both err and be correct, as abundantly evidenced in Hanafi works. The qibla example illustrates: if a congregation prays, each performing ijtihad for the qibla, and someone knows his imam's qibla is different, his prayer is invalid because he considers the imam mistaken. If all directions were equally valid (as the musawwiba claim), such invalidity would not occur. This shows that the hanafi view recognizes error in ijtihad. The text then refutes a counter-argument: the one who errs in qibla does not repeat his prayer because he is not required to hit the exact Kaaba but only to strive with hope; the true objective is seeking Allah's face, and that is attained. The same reasoning applies to all ijtihad: the action itself, even if factually wrong, is considered correct in terms of the mujtahid's obligation. The Shafi'i position, presented next, holds that the seeker is required to hit the physical Kaaba, so error necessitates repetition. The passage then presents proofs: the Shafi'i side cites a hadith about the captives of Badr implying only Umar would survive because he was correct; the Hanafi side cites the hadith of Amr ibn al-As and the Quranic verse about judgment and knowledge. Finally, a narration from Ibn Mas'ud shows two sahabah gave different rulings in a prayer scenario, and he affirmed both were correct, but preferred one—demonstrating that while error is possible, both are considered valid in terms of their ijtihad.

Key Terms
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): A qualified jurist who exerts effort to derive a ruling from legal sources.
الخطأ (al-khata'): In ijtihad, erring from the actual objective (e.g., missing the true qibla) while fulfilling the obligation of striving.
التحري (al-taharrin): The act of seeking and exerting effort to determine the correct direction (e.g., qibla) or legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what is the status of a person who performs ijtihad for the qibla and faces a direction other than the actual Kaaba?
A) His prayer is invalid and he must repeat it.
B) His prayer is valid but he is considered mistaken in his ijtihad.
C) He is not considered a mujtahid.
D) His ijtihad is invalid because he missed the Kaaba.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid's duty is to exert effort (taharrin), not to guarantee hitting the objective; error is possible and does not invalidate his action.

The Hanafi Doctrine of Takhti': Only One Mujtahid is Correct
Usul dispute: Taswib vs Takhti' of mujtahids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on Taswib and Takhti', identify their key proofs, and critically evaluate the Mu'tazili argument using the Hanafi responses.

Core Idea
Only one mujtahid reaches the true ruling; others are mistaken yet rewarded — ijtihad does not guarantee correctness.

The question of whether every mujtahid is correct (taswib) or only one is correct and others mistaken (takhti') is a foundational usul dispute that affects how we view conflicting legal opinions. The Hanafi school sides with takhti', arguing that ijtihad is rewarded effort, not a guarantee of hitting the divine ruling. In this passage, al-Bazdawi presents the Mu'tazili arguments and the Hanafi responses, concluding with the position that a mujtahid can be both correct and mistaken in different senses, as a precaution against Mu'tazili doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two doctrines
Define Taswib (every mujtahid is correct) and Takhti' (only one is correct). Note which school holds which.
Hanafis hold Takhti'; Mu'tazila hold Taswib.
2
Examine the Mu'tazili proofs
Identify the two arguments: (1) the hadith that uses 'mistake' in a general sense, and (2) the Badr incident where the Prophet followed Abu Bakr's advice despite a later verse suggesting it was a mistake.
The text says 'فقد احتج بما روينا من إطلاق الخطأ في الحديث' and 'وبقول النبي في أُسارى بدر'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
List the four Hanafi proofs: (1) hadith of Amr ibn al-As about the judge's reward, (2) Quran 21:79 ('And each We gave judgment and knowledge'), (3) the statement of Ibn Mas'ud that both companions hit the mark but one was preferred, (4) rational argument that the mujtahid is only tasked with his best effort.
The text says 'واحتج أصحابنا بحديث عمرو بن العاص وبقول الله تعالى: ﴿وَكُلًّا اَنَيْنَا حُكْمًا وَعِلْمًا﴾'.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to Badr
The Hanafis argue that the Badr incident was a concession (rukhsah), not an error; the verse refers to the strict ruling. A mistaken mujtahid is not blamed unless the truth is obvious.
The text says 'فقد عمل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بإشارة أبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه، فكيف يكون خطأ؟! إلا أن هذا كان رخصةً'.
5
Evaluate the link to Mu'tazili doctrine
Understand why Taswib leads to Mu'tazili beliefs: it implies the obligation of al-aslah (the optimal) and likens the guardian to the Prophet. The Hanafi chosen expression is to say the mujtahid is correct and mistaken in different respects, to avoid Mu'tazili tendencies.
The text says 'وإنما نسبنا القول بتعدد الحقوق إلى المعتزلة؛ لقولهم بوجوب الأصلح' and 'والمختار من العبارات عندنا أن يقال: إن المجتهد يُصيب ويخطئ على تحقيق المراد به'.

ولو كان كل مجتهد مصيبا : لسقطت المحنة، وبطل الاجتهاد.

ويتصل بهذا الأصل: مسألة تخصيص العلل، وهذا: باب فساد تخصيص العلل :
As for the one who considers him mistaken from beginning to end: he argued with what we have narrated of the absolute use of 'mistake' in the hadith, and with the statement of the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم regarding the captives of Badr when the verse was revealed: 'If not for a prior decree from Allah, a severe punishment would have touched you...' (Al-Anfal 8:68). And our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) argued with the hadith of Amr ibn al-As (may Allah be pleased with him) and with Allah's statement: 'And each [prophet] We gave judgment and knowledge' (Al-Anbiya 21:79). Judgment and knowledge are intended for action; as for hitting the mark, that is from one of them. And Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said to Masruq and al-Aswad: 'Each of you has hit the mark, and Masruq's practice is more beloved to me regarding the two rak'ahs of Maghrib they had missed.' And because every mujtahid is tasked with what is within his capacity, so he deserves reward for his initial action, and is deprived of correctness and reward in its outcome either due to a shortcoming from him, or due to deprivation from Allah initially. As for the story of Badr: the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم acted on the suggestion of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, so how could it be a mistake? Rather, this was a concession, and the intended meaning of the verse is according to the ruling of strictness; were it not for the concession, the mistaken one in this matter is not considered misguided nor rebuked unless the path of correctness is clear, in which case he is rebuked. - And we only attribute the statement of multiple correct opinions to the Mu'tazila because of their doctrine of the obligation of the optimal, and in the view that every mujtahid is correct: the obligation of holding to the optimal, and that the guardian is likened to the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم, and this is exactly their school. The chosen expression according to us is to say: The mujtahid is correct and mistaken in terms of what is intended, as a precaution against the Mu'tazila both outwardly and inwardly. On this we found our teachers, and on this the early companions proceeded. And Allah knows best. And if every mujtahid were correct, tribulation would be void and ijtihad would be invalid. Connected to this principle is the issue of specification of causes, and this is the chapter on the invalidity of specification of causes.
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a classic usul al-fiqh dispute regarding the status of a mujtahid: whether every mujtahid is correct (taswib) or only one is correct and others are mistaken (takhti'). The Hanafi school adopts the latter view, contrary to the Mu'tazila who hold that every mujtahid is correct. The passage begins by presenting the argument of those who consider the mistaken mujtahid as erring both in initial intent and result: they cite the general use of 'mistake' in hadith and the incident of Badr where the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم acted on the suggestion of Abu Bakr, implying that even the Prophet's companions could have been mistaken. The Hanafis then respond with their proofs: the hadith of Amr ibn al-As (likely the hadith 'When a judge makes a ruling and strives, if he is correct he has two rewards; if he errs, he has one reward') and the Quranic verse 'And each [prophet] We gave judgment and knowledge' (Al-Anbiya 21:79), arguing that judgment and knowledge are for action, not necessarily hitting the objective; hitting the mark comes from one of them. They also cite the incident of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud with Masruq and al-Aswad, where he affirmed both as correct in their ijtihad but preferred one, showing that both can be rewarded but only one is correct. They add a rational argument: a mujtahid is only obligated to do his best, so he deserves reward for his effort, but correctness of the outcome is not guaranteed; it is either due to his own shortcoming or divine withholding. Regarding the Badr incident, they clarify that the Prophet's action was a concession, and the verse refers to the strict ruling; a mistaken mujtahid is not rebuked unless the right path is clear. The passage then links the doctrine of taswib to the Mu'tazila, arguing that it implies the obligation of the optimal (al-aslah) and likening the guardian (wali) to the Prophet, which is their core belief. The Hanafi chosen position is to state that the mujtahid is both correct and mistaken in terms of what is intended, guarding against Mu'tazili tendencies. Finally, an argument against taswib: if every mujtahid were correct, there would be no tribulation (imtihan) and ijtihad would be pointless. The passage ends by connecting this to the upcoming topic: specification of causes.

Key Terms
تصويب (taswib): The doctrine that every mujtahid is correct in his ijtihad and that there is no single correct answer.
تخطئة (takhti'): The doctrine that only one mujtahid is correct and others are mistaken, though they may be rewarded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the status of a mujtahid who exerts effort but does not reach the correct ruling?
A) He is sinful and his ruling is invalid.
B) He is correct just like the one who reached the truth.
C) He is mistaken but rewarded for his ijtihad.
D) He is considered infallible in his ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that only one mujtahid is correct; others are mistaken but rewarded, and taswib is rejected as it invalidates ijtihad and aligns with Mu'tazili doctrine.

Mumana'a fi al-Shurut: Conditions for Objecting to Ta'lil
Hanafi rebuttal of Shafi'i examples using conditions of ta'lil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of mumana'a by identifying when a condition is agreed upon by consensus and distinguishing between form and meaning in legal objections.

Core Idea
A valid objection to ta'lil requires the absence of a condition agreed upon by consensus; mere description without effect is not proof.

In this passage, the Hanafi author responds to Imam al-Shafi'i's examples (false oath and salam) by clarifying the proper method of objecting to a causation (ta'lil). The key is that not any missing condition suffices—only those established by consensus. Moreover, the opponent must demonstrate the causal effect of the description, not just assert it. This lesson will guide you through the Hanafi framework for formulating or rebutting objections in legal analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i claim and the Hanafi response
Locate the examples of yamin al-ghamus and salam halal. Understand that the Shafi'i argues these share a description with another case, enabling analogy. The Hanafi response rejects this because the conditions of ta'lil are violated.
2
Determine the condition for valid mumana'a
According to the Hanafi author, a valid objection requires that a condition of ta'lil, which is agreed upon by consensus (bil-ijma'), be absent in the branch or original. Not any condition—only those universally accepted.
This narrows the scope of valid objections.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i examples against Hanafi conditions
The author states that two conditions are violated: (1) ta'lil must not change a textual ruling, and (2) the original must not deviate from analogy. Apply these to the examples and see why they fail.
Maps to the condition that ta'lil must preserve the text and analogy.
4
Understand the requirement of athar (effect)
The Hanafi school insists that a mere description (wasf) without a demonstrated legal effect (athar) is not a valid cause. The opponent must show how the description produces the ruling.
This is a core Hanafi difference from other schools.
5
Distinguish between form and meaning in denial
The author notes that denial in this context is considered in meaning, not form—like the trustee's claim of return being accepted despite being a claimant in form. This reinforces that substantive analysis trumps procedural labels.
Helps understand the depth of the Hanafi method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الممانعة في الشروط : فقد ذكرنا شروط التعليل، وإنما يجب يمنع شرطاً منها ما هو شرط بالإجماع ، وقد عُدم في الفرع والأصل. مثل قول الشافعي رحمه الله في السّلم الحال: إنه أحد عوضي البيع، فيثبت حالاً ومؤجلاً، كثمن المبيع. فيقال له: لا خلاف أن من شرط صحة التعليل أن لا يُغير حكم النص، وأن لا يكون الأصلُ معدولاً به عن القياس بحكمه، وإنا لا نسلم هذا الشرط ها هنا. وأما الممانعةُ في المعنى الذي به صار دليلاً فهو ما ذكرنا من الأثر؛ لأن مجرد الوصف بلا أثر ليس بحجة عندنا، فلا يصح الاحتجاج به من الخصم على من لا يراه حجةً ودليلاً حتى يبين أثره، وسبيله في هذا الباب كله الإنكار، وإنما يُعتبر الإنكار معنى، لا صورةً، مثل قولنا في المودَع يدعي الردَّ : إن القول قوله، وهو مدع صورةً، والله تعالى أعلم.
And as for objection (mumana'a) in the conditions: we have mentioned the conditions of causation (ta'lil), and it is only necessary that what is a condition by consensus be absent in the branch and the original. For example, the saying of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, regarding the oath of falsehood (yamin al-ghamus): that it is binding (ma'quda). And regarding salam (advance payment) for immediate delivery: that it is one of the two counter-values of a sale, so it is established as immediate and deferred, like the price of an item sold. So it is said to him: There is no disagreement that among the conditions for validity of causation is that it should not change the ruling of the text, and that the original should not be deviated from analogy by its ruling. And we do not accept this condition here. As for objection in the meaning by which it becomes an evidence (dalil), it is what we mentioned of the effect (athar); because a mere description without effect is not a proof (hujja) according to us, so it is not valid for the opponent to argue with it against one who does not consider it a proof and evidence until he demonstrates its effect. And his way in this entire chapter is mere denial, and denial is considered in meaning, not form, like our saying: The trustee (wadi') who claims return (radd): his statement is accepted, and he is a claimant in form. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi rebuttal to Shafi'i arguments in the context of ta'lil conditions. The Hanafi author clarifies that for a valid objection (mumana'a) against a legal analogy, one must show that a condition of causation—specifically a condition agreed upon by consensus—is absent in the branch (far') or the original (asl). He gives two examples from Imam al-Shafi'i: first, the binding nature of a false oath (yamin al-ghamus), and second, the permissibility of deferring the price in a salam contract both immediately and later. The Hanafi response is that such arguments fail because they violate the condition that ta'lil must not alter a textual ruling or depart from analogical reasoning. Furthermore, the author emphasizes that mere description of a property (wasf) without demonstrating its effective cause (athar) does not constitute proof in the Hanafi school. The opponent must show the causal effect. This is a nuanced discussion on the methodology of qiyas and the requirements for valid opposition.

Key Terms
الممانعة (al-mumana'a): Objection or contradiction in the context of legal analogy; challenging the validity of ta'lil by pointing out missing conditions.
شروط التعليل (shurut al-ta'lil): Conditions for valid causation that must be met in qiyas; e.g., not changing textual ruling and not deviating from analogy.
الأثر (al-athar): The legal effect or impact of a cause (wasf) that justifies the ruling; a mere description without effect is not a valid cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi author, what is necessary for a valid objection (mumana'a) against a causation (ta'lil)?
A) That any condition is missing
B) That a condition agreed upon by consensus is missing in both branch and original
C) That the opponent fails to provide an example
D) That the textual ruling is changed

Key Takeaway
A valid objection to a causation in qiyas requires that a condition agreed upon by consensus be absent, and that the description have a proven legal effect; form does not substitute for substance.
