Slavery's Effect on Marriage: Only Halving, Not Prohibition
Analyzing the legal effect of slavery on marriage capacity in dispute with al-Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that slavery halves countable legal rulings without creating additional prohibitions, and distinguish this from the Shafi'i position on marrying Kitabi slave women.

Core Idea
Slavery halves the permissible; it never prohibits what is already allowed for free persons.

This lesson examines a fundamental usul dispute: does slavery, combined with disbelief, create a new impediment to marriage? The Hanafi school insists that slavery's legal effect is limited to halving numerical limits (e.g., number of wives, 'idda), not generating prohibitions. We will explore how this principle is applied to the case of a Muslim man marrying a Kitabi slave woman, contrasting it with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
What is the exact fiqh issue? The permissibility of a Muslim (free or slave) man marrying a Kitabi (Scripture) slave woman. Is it allowed or prohibited?
The dispute centres on whether the combination of slavery and disbelief creates a new impediment.
2
State the Hanafi position and its underlying principle
The Hanafi school holds it permissible. The principle: slavery (riqq) only 'halves' what is permissible for a free person (e.g., number of wives, 'idda), but does not impose any new prohibition. Disbelief alone does not prohibit marriage to a free Kitabi woman, so it does not prohibit marrying a Kitabi slave woman either.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i counterargument
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that slavery is a 'impediment' (mani') and disbelief is an impediment; when combined, they create a strong prohibition, attaching it to 'severe disbelief'. Moreover, the need for a slave woman is removed by the availability of Muslim slave women.
Shafi'i uses the idea that necessity (darura) for marrying a slave woman is no longer present.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: slavery's effect is one of halving, not prohibition; its effect is 'weak' (da'if) in creating prohibition. The religion of the Kitabi is valid for marriage, so it extends to slave women. The Shafi'i argument that necessity has ended is refuted: marrying a Kitabi slave woman is not obligatory but recommended, just like marrying a free Kitabi woman. The simultaneous application of slavery and disbelief does not transform into a new cause.
The text describes the opposing view as 'weak in reality and weak in conditions'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنكحة، وهذا قوي الأثر؛ لأن الحرية من صفات الكمال، وأسباب الكرامة، والرق من أسباب تنصيف الحل، فيجب أن يكون الرقيق في النصف مثل الحر في الكل فأما أن يزداد أثر الرق، ويتسع حله: فلا، وهذا أثر ظهرت قوته ويزداد وضوحاً بالتأمل في أحوال البشر. ألا يُرى أنه أُحِلَّ لرسولنا عليه الصلاة والسلام التسع، أو إلى ما لا يتناهى ؛ لفضله وشرفه فأما ما ذكر من الأثر فضعيف بحقيقته ؛ لأن الإرقاق دون التضييع، وذلك جائز بالعزل بإذن الحرة، فالإرقاق أولى ، وضعيف بأحواله، فإن نكاح الأمة جائز لمن يملك سُرية يستغني بها عنه. ومن ذلك قولهم في نكاح الأمة الكتابية : إنه لا يجوز للمسلم؛ لأن الرق من الموانع. وكذلك الكفر، فإذا اجتمعا ألحق بالكفر الغليظ، ولأن الضرورة انقضت بإحلال الأمة المسلمة. وقلنا نحن لا بأس به؛ لأنه دين يصح معه نكاح الحرة، فكذلك نكاح الأمة، كدين الإسلام، وهو نكاح يملكه العبد المسلم، وهذا أثر ظهرت قوتُه ؛ لما قلنا إن أثر الرق في التنصيف فيما يقبله. العدد كما قيل في الطلاق والعدة والقسم ،والحدود، وذلك يختص بما يقبل الأحكام، ونكاحُ المرأة في نفسه مقابلاً بالرجال ليس بمتعدد، فلا يحتمل التنصيف، لكنه ذو أحوال متعددة، وهي التقدم والتأخر والمقارنة، فيصح متقدماً، ولم يصح متأخراً قولاً بالتنصيف، وبطل مقارناً؛ لأنه لا يحتمل التنصيف، فغُلَّب التحريم، كالطلاق الثلاث، والأقراء أنها صارت ثنتين بالرق؛ لما قلنا، فهذا وصف قَوِيَ أثره. ولذلك قلنا في الحر إذا نكح أمةً على أمة : إنه صحيح، كالعبد إذا فَعَلَهُ، وضَعُفَ أثرُ وصفه؛ لأن الرق ليس من أسباب التحريم، لكنه من أسباب التنصيف، كرف الرجال لم يُحرِّم على الرجل شيئاً حل للحر، لكنه في التنصيف، وقد جعل الرقُ من أسباب فضل الحل، وهذا عكس المعقول، ونَقض الأصول. أثر ودين الكتابي ليس من أسباب التحريم أيضاً، وأثرهما مختلف أيضاً، فلا يصلح أن يُجعلا علةً واحدةً. وغيرُ مسلَّم له أن يكون نكاح الأمة في حكم الجواز ضرورياً، لكنه في حكم الاستحباب، مثلُ نكاح الحرة الكتابية؛ لما قلنا من سقوط حرمة الإرقاق. ومثاله أيضاً : ما قال الشافعي رحمه الله في إسلام أحد الزوجين : إنه أسباب الفرقة عند انقضاء العدة لا بنفسه، فكذلك الردة سوى بينهما، وهذا وصف ضعيف الأثر، لا يخفى على أحد. وقلنا نحن إن الإسلام ليس من أسباب الفرقة؛ لأنه من أسباب العصمة، وبقاء الآخر على ما كان ليس من أسبابه أيضاً، بالإجماع، فوجب إثبات الحكم مضافاً إلى سبب جديد، وهو فوات أغراض النكاح أداء الإسلام مضافاً إلى امتناع الآخر عن حقاً للذي أسلم، وهو سبب
Marriages, and this is a strong effect: because freedom is a quality of perfection and causes of honor, and slavery is a cause of halving the permissible, so it is necessary that the slave be in half like the free in whole. As for the effect of slavery increasing and its permissibility expanding: no. And this is an effect whose strength has appeared and becomes clearer by contemplation of human conditions. Do you not see that nine was made permissible for our Prophet (peace be upon him) or up to what does not end due to his merit and honor? As for what was mentioned of the effect, it is weak in its reality: because enslaving is not like wasting, and that is permissible by coitus interruptus with the permission of the free woman, so enslaving is more appropriate; and weak in its conditions, for marrying a slave woman is permissible for one who possesses a concubine to suffice him. And of that is their statement regarding marrying a Kitabi slave woman: that it is not permissible for a Muslim because slavery is among the impediments. Similarly, disbelief, so when they combine, it is attached to severe disbelief, and because the necessity ended by permitting the Muslim slave woman. And we said: there is no harm in it, because it is a religion with which marriage to a free woman is valid, so likewise marriage to a slave woman, like the religion of Islam, and it is a marriage that a Muslim slave can perform. And this is an effect whose strength appeared, because we said that the effect of slavery is in halving in what accepts it. Number, as was said in divorce, 'idda, division, and hadd punishments, and that is specific to what accepts rulings. And marriage of a woman in itself, compared to men, is not multiple, so it does not admit halving, but it has multiple states: precedence, delay, and simultaneity. So it is valid if preceding, and not valid if delayed according to halving, and void if simultaneous, because it does not admit halving, so prohibition prevails, like triple divorce, and the 'iddas that they become two by slavery, because we said. So this is a description whose effect is strong. And therefore we said regarding a free man if he marries a slave woman while having a slave woman: it is valid, like a slave if he does it; and the effect of his description is weak, because slavery is not a cause of prohibition, but it is a cause of halving, like the number of women does not prohibit for a man something permissible for a free man, but it is in halving. And he made slavery a cause of preference of permissibility, and this is contrary to reason and a violation of principles. The effect: and the religion of the Kitabi is not a cause of prohibition either, and their effects are different, so they cannot be made one cause. And it is not conceded to him that marrying a slave woman is necessary in the ruling of permissibility, but it is in the ruling of recommendation, like marrying a free Kitabi woman, because of what we said of dropping the prohibition of enslaving. And an example of that also: what al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding the Islam of one of the spouses: that it is a cause of separation at the end of the 'idda, not by itself, and similarly apostasy equates between them. And this is a weak description of effect, not hidden from anyone. And we said: Islam is not a cause of separation, because it is a cause of inviolability, and the remaining of the other on what he was is not a cause of it either, by consensus. So it is necessary to establish the ruling attributed to a new cause, which is the loss of the purposes of marriage due to the performance of Islam, added to the other's refusal of the right of the one who became Muslim, and that is a cause.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the legal effect (athar) of attributes like slavery (riqq) and disbelief (kufr) on marriage rulings. The author begins by stating the principle that slavery halves the permissible—it does not expand it. For example, a slave can marry only two wives (half of a free man's four). The author refutes the notion that slavery could increase permissibility, using the example of the Prophet's special permission for nine wives due to his honor, which does not apply to others. He then addresses the claim that marrying a Kitabi (Scripture) slave woman is impermissible for a Muslim because slavery is an impediment. The Hanafi response is that it is permissible because the religion of the Kitabi is valid for marriage to a free woman, so it is also valid for a slave woman, analogous to Islam. The author argues that the effect of slavery is limited to halving matters that can be halved (like number of wives, divorce, 'idda, etc.) and does not extend to prohibition. He gives the example of a free man marrying a slave woman while he already has a slave woman: it is valid because slavery does not cause prohibition, only halving. He criticizes the opposing view as contrary to reason and principles. Finally, he discusses the effect of Islam and apostasy on marriage, rebutting Shafi'i's view that Islam upon one spouse causes separation at the end of the waiting period. The Hanafi position is that Islam strengthens the marital bond ('isma); the separation occurs due to a new cause—the loss of marital purposes because the Muslim spouse demands what the non-Muslim refuses. The passage emphasizes consistency in the strength of legal effects.

Key Terms
أثر (athar): The legal effect or consequence of a description or cause (like slavery) on rulings.
تنصيف (tansif): Halving; the reduction of a legal quantity (e.g., number of wives) by half due to a cause like slavery.
رق (riqq): Slavery; a legal status that reduces capacity and halves certain rights and obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is prohibited because the Kitabi slave woman is not allowed to have sexual relations with a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Slavery only halves countable legal rulings, it never creates new prohibitions; Kitabi slave women are permissible for marriage just like free Kitabi women.

Islam Causes Inviolability, Not Separation: Rebutting the Analogy with Apostasy
Analyzing the legal effect of spousal conversion on marriage continuity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi usul considers Islam a cause of 'isma (inviolability) and refutes the Shafi'i analogy between Islam and apostasy in ending marriage.

Core Idea
Islam strengthens marriage; separation is due to a new cause, not the conversion itself.

This part of the passage addresses a critical usul point: does the Islam of one spouse automatically end the marriage, similar to apostasy? Imam al-Shafi'i argued yes, but the Hanafi school disagrees, rejecting the analogy. The Hanafi position insists that Islam is a cause of inviolability (isma), and any separation must be attributed to a new cause – the loss of marital purposes. We will unpack this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The issue: What is the legal effect of one spouse embracing Islam on an existing marriage? Does it cause separation, and if so, when?
2
State the Shafi'i position and its analogy
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that Islam of one spouse is a cause (sabab) of separation at the end of the 'idda (waiting period), not instantly. He draws an analogy: just as apostasy causes separation (instantly according to some, or at the end of 'idda according to the Shafi'i view), so does Islam.
The passage says: 'it is a cause of separation at the end of the 'idda, not by itself' – similar to apostasy.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that Islam is a cause of 'isma (inviolability/protection), not separation. The marriage bond is strengthened, not weakened. Separation does not occur unless a new cause arises – such as the loss of marital purposes (fawt aghrad al-nikah) when the Muslim spouse demands the other's conversion and the other refuses.
The Hanafi position relies on consensus: Islam is a cause of 'isma.
4
Evaluate the weakness of the Shafi'i analogy
The analogy between Islam and apostasy fails because apostasy nullifies the marriage contract (due to change of religion), whereas Islam does not change the religion of the couple in a way that breaks the contract – rather, it adds inviolability. The author calls the Shafi'i analogy 'a weak description of effect'.

Source Text

الأنكحة، وهذا قوي الأثر؛ لأن الحرية من صفات الكمال، وأسباب الكرامة، والرق من أسباب تنصيف الحل، فيجب أن يكون الرقيق في النصف مثل الحر في الكل فأما أن يزداد أثر الرق، ويتسع حله: فلا، وهذا أثر ظهرت قوته بالعزل بإذن الحرة، فالإرقاق أولى ، وضعيف بأحواله، فإن نكاح الأمة جائز لمن يملك سُرية يستغني بها عنه. ومن ذلك قولهم في نكاح الأمة الكتابية : إنه لا يجوز للمسلم؛ لأن الرق من الموانع. وكذلك الكفر، فإذا اجتمعا ألحق بالكفر الغليظ، ولأن الضرورة انقضت بإحلال الأمة المسلمة. وقلنا نحن لا بأس به؛ لأنه دين يصح معه نكاح الحرة، فكذلك نكاح الأمة، كدين الإسلام، وهو نكاح يملكه العبد المسلم، وهذا أثر ظهرت قوتُه ؛ لما قلنا إن أثر الرق في التنصيف فيما يقبله. العدد كما قيل في الطلاق والعدة والقسم ،والحدود، وذلك يختص بما يقبل الأحكام، ونكاحُ المرأة في نفسه مقابلاً بالرجال ليس بمتعدد، فلا يحتمل التنصيف، لكنه ذو قلنا، فهذا وصف قَوِيَ أثره. ولذلك قلنا في الحر إذا نكح أمةً على أمة : إنه صحيح، كالعبد إذا فَعَلَهُ، وضَعُفَ أثرُ وصفه؛ لأن الرق ليس من أسباب التحريم، لكنه من أسباب التنصيف، كرف الرجال لم يُحرِّم على الرجل شيئاً حل للحر، لكنه في التنصيف، وقد جعل الرقُ من أسباب فضل الحل، وهذا عكس المعقول، ونَقض الأصول. أثر ودين الكتابي ليس من أسباب التحريم أيضاً، وأثرهما مختلف أيضاً، فلا يصلح أن يُجعلا علةً واحدةً. وغيرُ مسلَّم له أن يكون نكاح الأمة في حكم الجواز ضرورياً، لكنه في حكم الاستحباب، مثلُ نكاح الحرة الكتابية؛ لما قلنا من سقوط حرمة الإرقاق. ومثاله أيضاً : ما قال الشافعي رحمه الله في إسلام أحد الزوجين : إنه أسباب الفرقة عند انقضاء العدة لا بنفسه، فكذلك الردة سوى بينهما، وهذا وصف ضعيف الأثر، لا يخفى على أحد. وقلنا نحن إن الإسلام ليس من أسباب الفرقة؛ لأنه من أسباب العصمة، وبقاء الآخر على ما كان ليس من أسبابه أيضاً، بالإجماع، فوجب إثبات الحكم مضافاً إلى سبب جديد، وهو فوات أغراض النكاح أداء الإسلام مضافاً إلى امتناع الآخر عن حقاً للذي أسلم، وهو سبب
Marriages, and this is a strong effect: because freedom is a quality of perfection and causes of honor, and slavery is a cause of halving the permissible, so it is necessary that the slave be in half like the free in whole. As for the effect of slavery increasing and its permissibility expanding: no. And this is an effect whose strength has appeared and becomes clearer by contemplation of human conditions. Do you not see that nine was made permissible for our Prophet (peace be upon him) or up to what does not end due to his merit and honor? As for what was mentioned of the effect, it is weak in its reality: because enslaving is not like wasting, and that is permissible by coitus interruptus with the permission of the free woman, so enslaving is more appropriate; and weak in its conditions, for marrying a slave woman is permissible for one who possesses a concubine to suffice him. And of that is their statement regarding marrying a Kitabi slave woman: that it is not permissible for a Muslim because slavery is among the impediments. Similarly, disbelief, so when they combine, it is attached to severe disbelief, and because the necessity ended by permitting the Muslim slave woman. And we said: there is no harm in it, because it is a religion with which marriage to a free woman is valid, so likewise marriage to a slave woman, like the religion of Islam, and it is a marriage that a Muslim slave can perform. And this is an effect whose strength appeared, because we said that the effect of slavery is in halving in what accepts it. Number, as was said in divorce, 'idda, division, and hadd punishments, and that is specific to what accepts rulings. And marriage of a woman in itself, compared to men, is not multiple, so it does not admit halving, but it has multiple states: precedence, delay, and simultaneity. So it is valid if preceding, and not valid if delayed according to halving, and void if simultaneous, because it does not admit halving, so prohibition prevails, like triple divorce, and the 'iddas that they become two by slavery, because we said. So this is a description whose effect is strong. And therefore we said regarding a free man if he marries a slave woman while having a slave woman: it is valid, like a slave if he does it; and the effect of his description is weak, because slavery is not a cause of prohibition, but it is a cause of halving, like the number of women does not prohibit for a man something permissible for a free man, but it is in halving. And he made slavery a cause of preference of permissibility, and this is contrary to reason and a violation of principles. The effect: and the religion of the Kitabi is not a cause of prohibition either, and their effects are different, so they cannot be made one cause. And it is not conceded to him that marrying a slave woman is necessary in the ruling of permissibility, but it is in the ruling of recommendation, like marrying a free Kitabi woman, because of what we said of dropping the prohibition of enslaving. And an example of that also: what al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding the Islam of one of the spouses: that it is a cause of separation at the end of the 'idda, not by itself, and similarly apostasy equates between them. And this is a weak description of effect, not hidden from anyone. And we said: Islam is not a cause of separation, because it is a cause of inviolability, and the remaining of the other on what he was is not a cause of it either, by consensus. So it is necessary to establish the ruling attributed to a new cause, which is the loss of the purposes of marriage due to the performance of Islam, added to the other's refusal of the right of the one who became Muslim, and that is a cause.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the legal effect (athar) of attributes like slavery (riqq) and disbelief (kufr) on marriage rulings. The author begins by stating the principle that slavery halves the permissible—it does not expand it. For example, a slave can marry only two wives (half of a free man's four). The author refutes the notion that slavery could increase permissibility, using the example of the Prophet's special permission for nine wives due to his honor, which does not apply to others. He then addresses the claim that marrying a Kitabi (Scripture) slave woman is impermissible for a Muslim because slavery is an impediment. The Hanafi response is that it is permissible because the religion of the Kitabi is valid for marriage to a free woman, so it is also valid for a slave woman, analogous to Islam. The author argues that the effect of slavery is limited to halving matters that can be halved (like number of wives, divorce, 'idda, etc.) and does not extend to prohibition. He gives the example of a free man marrying a slave woman while he already has a slave woman: it is valid because slavery does not cause prohibition, only halving. He criticizes the opposing view as contrary to reason and principles. Finally, he discusses the effect of Islam and apostasy on marriage, rebutting Shafi'i's view that Islam upon one spouse causes separation at the end of the waiting period. The Hanafi position is that Islam strengthens the marital bond ('isma); the separation occurs due to a new cause—the loss of marital purposes because the Muslim spouse demands what the non-Muslim refuses. The passage emphasizes consistency in the strength of legal effects.

Key Terms
عصمة (isma): Inviolability; a divine protection that strengthens the marriage bond; the opposite of separation.
فوات أغراض النكاح (fawt aghrad al-nikah): The loss of the purposes of marriage; a new cause that may lead to separation when one spouse converts and the other refuses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It automatically dissolves the marriage at the end of the 'idda.
B) It strengthens the marriage and does not cause separation unless a new cause (like loss of marital purposes) arises.
C) It dissolves the marriage immediately.
D) It depends on whether the other spouse also converts within the 'idda.

Key Takeaway
Islam strengthens the marriage bond; separation after a spouse's conversion is due to a new cause, not the conversion itself.

Tarjih of Hanafi Descriptions: Rukn, Specificity, and Liability
Argues for Hanafi definitions using logical and scriptural proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi method of tarjih by analyzing how al-Bazdawi defends the descriptions of rukn, muta'ayyin, and daman against opposing positions.

Core Idea
Hanafi definitions are preferred because they avoid logical fallacies and extend coherently across legal chapters.

This passage from al-Bazdawi’s *Kanz al-Wusul* presents a series of comparisons (tarjih) where the Hanafi school’s characterizations of legal concepts are argued to be more appropriate than those of its opponents. By examining three key examples—the definition of rukn in prayer, the specificity of Ramadan fasting, and the guarantee of benefits—the author demonstrates a method of weighing descriptions based on logical consistency and broader fiqh implications. Understanding this method sharpens your ability to evaluate usul arguments and discern the preferred position within scholarly disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Descriptions
Locate the three Hanafi claims being defended: (a) rukn is fulfilled by lengthening, not repetition; (b) the fast of Ramadan is muta'ayyin (specific), not merely fard; (c) benefits are not guaranteed by destruction.
Each claim contrasts with an opposing description.
2
Understand the Opponents' Position
Infer what the opponents say: they likely define rukn differently, call the fast merely fard, and argue that what is guaranteed by contract is also guaranteed by destruction.
The text does not name the opponent but implies a systematic alternative.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Reasoning (Dalil Aqli)
For each claim, examine the logical argument: (a) repetition does not define rukn because rinsing and sniffing are repeated but not rukn; (b) specificity is necessary because it extends to deposits, usurpations, etc.; (c) guaranteeing benefits would impose excess on the transgressor, which is unjust.
The reasoning is based on consistency and avoiding excess.
4
Evaluate the Tarjih (Preference)
Understand why the Hanafi description is called 'awla' (more appropriate): it avoids logical fallacies, aligns with broader legal principles, and prevents injustice.
The tarjih is not arbitrary but rooted in maqasid.
5
Apply the Method to Other Examples
Consider how this method of tarjih (comparing descriptions for logical fit) can be used in other usul disputes, e.g., definitions of 'ibadat vs. mu'amalat.
The goal is to internalize the reasoning process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قضية الركن إكماله بالإطالة في الركوع والسجود، لا تكراره، ووجدنا في الباب ما ليس بركن ويتكرر، وهو المضمضة والاستنشاق. وأما أثر المسح في التخفيف فثابت لازم، لا محالة في كل ما لا الخف يُعقل تطهيراً، كالتيمم، ومسح الجبائر، ومسح الجوارب. وكذلك قولنا في صوم رمضان إنه متعين: أولى من قولهم : صومٌ فرض؛ لأن الفرضية لا توجب إلا الامتثال به، لا التعيين، لا محالة، وذلك وصف خاص في الباب، وأما التعيين فلازم، حتى تعدى إلى الودائع والغُصوب ورد البيع الفاسد وعقد الأيمان ونحوها، فكان أولى. وكذلك قولنا في المنافع إنها لا تُضمن مراعاة لشرط ضمان العدوان بالاحتراز عن الفضل أولى من قولهم: إن ما يُضمن بالعقد: يُضمن بالإتلاف؛ تحقيقاً للجبر، وإثبات المثل تقريباً وإن كان فيه إيجاب فضل ; لأنه فضل على المتعدي أو إهدار على المظلوم، ولأنه إهدار وصف، أو إهدار أصل، فكان الأول أولى؛ لأن التقييد بالمثل واجب في كل باب كما في الأموال كلها، والصيام والصلاة وغيرها. ووَضعُ الضمان عن المعصوم أمر جائز مثل العادل يُتلف مال الباغي، والحربي يُتلف مال المسلم، والفضل على المتعدي غير مشروع، وهذا لأنه وإن قل فإنه حكم شرعي يُنسب إلى صاحب الشرع بلا واسطة ونسبةُ الجَوْر إليه بدون واسطة فعل العبد : باطل، وأن لا يُضمن مضاف إلى عجزنا عن الدَّرْك، وذلك سائغ حسن. ولأن الوصف وإن قل فائتُ أصلاً بلا بدل، والأصل وإن عظم فائتُ إلى ضمان في دار الجزاء، فكان تأخيراً، والأول إبطالاً، والتأخير أهون من الإبطال وهذا كذلك في عامة الأحكام، فأما ضمان العقد : فباب خاص، فكان ما قلناه أولى.
The issue of the pillar is its completion by lengthening in bowing and prostration, not its repetition, and we found in the chapter what is not a pillar and is repeated, which is rinsing the mouth and sniffing water. As for the effect of wiping in alleviation, it is firmly established and necessary, inevitably in everything that the khuff is not rationally considered purification, such as tayammum, wiping over splints, and wiping over socks. Similarly, our statement regarding the fast of Ramadan that it is specific is more appropriate than their statement: 'a fast that is obligatory' because obligation does not necessitate anything except compliance with it, not specificity, inevitably, and that is a specific description in the chapter, and specificity is necessary, to the extent that it extends to deposits, usurpations, returning a void sale, the contract of oaths, and the like, so it is more appropriate. Similarly, our statement regarding benefits that they are not guaranteed, observing the condition of guarantee of transgression by avoiding excess, is more appropriate than their statement: 'what is guaranteed by contract is guaranteed by destruction' – to achieve compensation, and establishing the equivalent approximately even if it involves imposing excess, because it is excess upon the transgressor or nullification upon the oppressed, and because it is nullification of a description or nullification of the essence, so the first is more appropriate because restriction to the equivalent is obligatory in every chapter as in all properties, fasting, prayer, and others. And placing guarantee away from the protected is permissible, such as the just person destroying the property of the rebel, and the warring person destroying the property of the Muslim, and excess upon the transgressor is not permitted, and this is because even if it is small, it is a legal ruling attributed to the Lawgiver without intermediary, and attributing injustice to Him without the intermediary of the slave's action is false, and that it is not guaranteed is attributed to our inability to recover, and that is permissible and good. And because the description even if small is lost originally without compensation, and the essence even if great is lost subject to guarantee in the abode of recompense, so it is a delay, and the first is nullification, and delay is easier than nullification, and this is so in all rulings. As for guarantee of contract, it is a special chapter, so what we said is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments from the Hanafi school (al-Bazdawi) to defend their specific descriptions and rulings against the opponents' alternative characterizations. The author uses logical and scriptural reasoning to show that the Hanafi position is more appropriate (awla) because it avoids fallacies and aligns with broader legal principles. The discussion covers four main examples: (1) The definition of 'rukn' (pillar) in prayer—Hanafis say it is fulfilled by lengthening in bowing and prostration, not repetition, citing that rinsing the mouth and sniffing water in wudu are repeated but not pillars. (2) The effect of wiping (e.g., on khuff) is a necessary alleviation, applicable to tayammum, splints, and socks. (3) The fast of Ramadan is 'specific' (muta'ayyin) rather than merely 'obligatory' (fard), because specificity extends to other rulings like deposits and usurpations. (4) Benefits (manafi') are not guaranteed because the principle of compensation for transgression should avoid imposing excess on the transgressor, even if it means some loss to the victim, as delaying compensation is easier than nullifying the original right. The author concludes that guarantee by contract is a separate matter, and their overall position is more sound.

Key Terms
الركن (al-rukn): A pillar of prayer, completed by lengthening (itwal) in bowing and prostration, not by repetition.
المتعين (al-muta'ayyin): Specific, as in the fast of Ramadan being specifically determined (as opposed to merely obligatory).
الضمان (al-daman): Guarantee or liability, especially for benefits (manafi') which are not guaranteed to avoid excess.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because specificity is a necessary description that extends to other rulings like deposits and usurpations.
C) Because the fast is only for one month, making it specific by nature.
D) Because the opponents also consider it specific.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method of tarjih emphasizes logical consistency and broad fiqh application, preferring descriptions that avoid excess and align with principles of justice.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Preponderance of Ghāsib's Work
Dispute on whether ghāsib's work overrides owner's right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tarjih (preponderance) when existence and continuation conflict, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Existence preponderates over continuation when two attributes conflict.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the preponderance of a ghāsib's work on an owner's property. The Hanafis argue that the work's independent existence cuts off the owner's right, while the Shafi'is maintain the owner remains entitled. The resolution hinges on the rational principle that existence is more fundamental than continuation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Articulate the Hanafi claim that the ghāsib's work severs the owner's right because the work has independent existence and the substance is perishing in one aspect.
The work is self-subsisting (qa'im bi-dhātiha).
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the owner of the original (ṣāḥib al-aṣl) is more entitled because the work remains subsidiary and attached to the product.
The Shafi'i argument: the work is subsidiary (tābi‘) to the manufactured object.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i reasoning: the work continues in the object, so the owner's right over the original substance endures.
They emphasize continuity (baqā') of the substance.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that continuation (baqā') is a state that exists only after existence (wujūd). When the attributes of existence and continuation conflict, existence must preponderate because it is more foundational.
Key phrase from text: 'al-wujūd aḥaqqu min al-baqā'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical fiqh consequence: in a case like a ghāsib sewing cloth into a garment, the owner cannot reclaim the garment without compensating the ghāsib for his labor.
This shows the usul principle directly impacts property rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا رحمهم الله في مسائل صنعة الغاصب في الخياطة والصياغة والطبخ والشي ونحوها إنه ينقطع حق المالك؛ لأن الصنعة قائمة بذاتها من كل وجه، ولا يضاف حدوثها إلى صاحب العين، وأما العين فهالكة من وجه، وهي من ذلك الوجه تُضاف إلى صنعة الغاصب، فصارت الصنعة راجحةً في الوجود. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : صاحب الأصل أحق؛ لأن الصنعة باقية بالمصنوع، تابعة له. والجواب عنه : ما قلنا إن البقاء حال بعد الوجود، فإذا تعارضا كان الوجود أحق من البقاء.
And on this our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said in the issues of the ghāsib's work in tailoring, crafting, cooking, roasting and the like: the owner's right is cut off; because the work is self-subsisting in every aspect, and its occurrence is not attributed to the owner of the substance, while the substance is perishing in one aspect, and in that aspect it is attributed to the ghāsib's work, so the work became preponderant in existence. And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: the owner of the original is more entitled, because the work remains in the manufactured object, subsidiary to it. The response to him: what we said that continuation is a state after existence, so when they conflict, existence is more entitled than continuation.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the preponderance (tarjih) when two attributes conflict: the existence of a new created work (ghāsib's labor) versus the continuation of the original substance. The Hanafi position is that the owner's right is severed because the work (e.g., sewing, dyeing) has an independent existence that is not attributed to the owner. The substance is considered non-existent from one aspect (since it has been transformed) and thus attributed to the ghāsib's work, making the work preponderant. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the owner of the original material is more entitled because the work is subsidiary and remains attached to the product. The Hanafi response is that continuation (baqā') is a state that occurs after existence, and when existence and continuation conflict, existence takes precedence. This is a rational argument based on the principle of 'wujūd ahaqqu min al-baqā'.

Key Terms
صنعة الغاصب (ṣan‘at al-ghāṣib): The work performed by a usurper on another's property, e.g., sewing, dyeing, cooking.
رجحان الذات (rujḥān al-dhāt): Preponderance of an attribute that is inherent (essence) over a temporal state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to al-Shafi'i's argument that the owner of the material is more entitled because the work remains attached?
A) The work is independent and not attributable to the owner.
B) The substance never perishes, so the owner is still entitled.
C) Continuation is a state after existence, and existence preponderates.
D) The ghāsib has no right at all.

Key Takeaway
When existence and continuation conflict, existence preponderates, which in the case of a ghāsib's work severs the owner's right to reclaim without compensation.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Intention Before Midday in Specific Fasts
Dispute on validity of intention before noon in specific fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi tarjih principle of 'preponderance by majority' to the issue of intention timing in fasting, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on precaution.

Core Idea
When determination exists in most of the act, the whole is valid – essence outweighs state.

This passage tackles a classic usul dispute: can the intention for a specific obligatory fast be made after dawn, up until noon? The Hanafis say yes, based on a rational preponderance argument that the fast is a single continuous act, and determination (ʿazīmah) exists for the greater part of the day. The Shafiʿis insist on precaution: no intention before dawn invalidates. The debate exposes how Hanafi usul prioritizes the essence of an act over a temporary state of deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the contested condition
The act is fasting from dawn to dusk. The condition in dispute is whether intention can be made after dawn but before noon, or must occur before dawn.
2
Understand the Hanafi reasoning
Hanafi: fasting is one continuous pillar; determination (ʿazīmah) exists from dawn to noon (the majority of the day), so it preponderates over the missing determination before dawn.
The majority of the day has determination, so the whole is valid.
3
Grasp the Shafi'i objection
Shafi'i: precaution (ihtiyat) demands that any lack of determination should render the whole invalid, so intention must be before dawn.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi response: preferring invalidity would 'abrogate the essence by a state' – you cannot nullify the whole continuous act just because a part lacks determination.
Essence overrides state.
5
Derive the practical difference
Hanafi: can intend until midday if forgot or woke late. Shafi'i: must intend before dawn.
This difference matters for millions of Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك على هذا قلنا في صوم رمضان وفي كل صومٍ عَيْنٍ: إنه يجوز بالنية قبل انتصاف النهار ؛ لأنه ركن واحد تعلق جوازه بالعزيمة، فإذا وجدت العزيمة في البعض دون البعض تعارضا، فرجحنا بالكثرة. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : بل يُرجّح الفساد؛ احتياطاً في العبادة. والجواب عنه : ما ذكرنا أن هذا يؤدي إلى نسخ الذات بالحال.
And likewise on this we said regarding fasting in Ramadan and every specific fast: it is permissible with the intention before the middle of the day, because it is one pillar whose permissibility is attached to determination (ʿazīmah), so when determination is found in some part but not in another, they conflict, and we give preponderance by majority. And al-Shafiʿi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: rather, invalidity should be preferred, as a precaution in worship. The response to him: what we mentioned that this leads to abrogating the essence by the state.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage continues the tarjih discussion applied to fasting. The Hanafi school holds that for Ramadan and any specific fast (e.g., vow, expiation), it is valid to make the intention before noon (midday). The reasoning is that fasting is a single pillar (a continuous act from dawn to dusk) whose permissibility depends on the presence of determination (ʿazīmah) at the time it is required. Determination exists in the part of the day from dawn to noon (since the fast is kept), but not in the part before dawn (since intention made then is not yet realized). These two parts conflict (one has determination, the other lacks it), so we give preponderance to the majority (the longer period with determination), thus the fast is valid. Imam al-Shafiʿi argues for precaution: partial lack of determination should invalidate the fast. The Hanafi response is that this would amount to 'abrogating the essence (the continuous act) by a state (partial lack)', which is invalid—the essence (the whole day) should not be overridden by a mere state of part of it.

Key Terms
صوم عين (ṣawm ʿayn): A specific fast determined by the day (e.g., Ramadan, vow).
عزيمة (ʿazīmah): Determination/firm resolve that validates intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is intention before noon valid for Ramadan?
A) Because the day is considered one continuous act and determination exists in the majority of it.
B) Because the Prophet ﷺ specifically allowed it.
C) Because precaution requires it.
D) Because intention can be made at any time.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the essence of a continuous act (like fasting) is not invalidated by a temporary state lacking determination if determination exists for the majority of the act.

General Intention and Temporal Specification in Ramadan Fast
Response to argument that Ramadan fast requires specified intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument that a general intention suffices for the Ramadan fast by analyzing the role of temporal specification (ta'yin bi al-waqt).

Core Idea
A general intention to fast in Ramadan is automatically specified by the time itself.

One of the key differences between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools concerns the necessity of explicitly verbalizing the intention for obligatory acts of worship. For the Ramadan fast, the Shafi'i school requires a specific intention (ta'yin an-niyya) such as 'I intend to fast tomorrow from Ramadan.' The Hanafi school, however, accepts a general intention (mutlaq) to fast on that day because the time of Ramadan itself specifies the fast. This passage presents the Hanafi logical proof for this position, anchored in the concept of description (wasf) and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opposing Argument
Recognize that the opponents argue: 'The Ramadan fast is obligatory (fard), therefore it is not valid without a specifically stated intention.' Extract the underlying logic: wajib acts require clear specification.
The opposing school is likely the Shafi'i madhhab.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafis agree that specification is necessary, but they disagree about how it is achieved. They claim that the description (wasf) of being fard 'necessitates specification' but 'does not prevent the existence of what specifies it.' Here, the timeframe of Ramadan itself becomes the specifying factor.
This is a critical distinction: the requirement does not dictate the method of specification.
3
Examine the Dalil (Proof)
The Hanafi proof is logical: since the obligation is tied to a specific time (Ramadan), intending to fast on any day of Ramadan inherently intends the Ramadan fast. Thus, a general (mutlaq) intention is actually a specified intention because the context provides the specification.
No additional verbalization is needed.
4
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: A Hanafi who wakes up and simply 'intends to fast today' during Ramadan has a valid fast. A Shafi'i would not consider the fast valid unless he explicitly says 'Ramadan.'
This difference affects millions of Muslims globally.
5
Apply the Usul Principle
Generalize the principle: when an obligation is tied to a specific time or context that uniquely identifies it, a general intention suffices. This reasoning can be applied to other time-specific acts like the Zuhr prayer.
This exemplifies how usul al-fiqh derives rulings from logical inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك قولهم في صوم رمضان : إنه صومٌ فرض، فلا يصح إلا بتعيين النية، فقلنا نحن بموجبه ؛ لأن هذا الوصف يوجب التعيين، لكنه لا يمنع وجود ما يعينه، فيكون إطلاقه تعييناً، ولأنه لا عندنا إلا بتعيين النية ؛ لأنا إنما نجوزه بإطلاق النية على أنه تعيين. يصح
And from that is their saying about fasting Ramadan: It is an obligatory fast, so it is not valid except with specification of intention. So we said in accordance with its requirement; because this description necessitates specification, but it does not prevent the existence of what specifies it, so its absoluteness becomes specification. And because according to us it is only with specification of intention, for we only permit it with absolute intention on the basis that it is specification. It is valid.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi response to opponents who argue that the Ramadan fast, being obligatory (fard), requires a specifically stated intention (ta'yin an-niyya). The Hanafis agree that specification is necessary, but they argue that a general (mutlaq) intention to fast for Ramadan is automatically specification because the time (Ramadan) itself specifies the fast. Thus, intending to fast on a day of Ramadan inherently means intending the Ramadan fast, so no separate verbal specification is needed. This is the Hanafi position that an absolute intention suffices.

Key Terms
تعيين النية (ta'yin an-niyya): Specification of intention; explicitly stating what one intends to do.
إطلاق النية (itlaq an-niyya): Absolute or general intention, not tied to a specific act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A general intention suffices for the Ramadan fast because the time of Ramadan inherently specifies it, demonstrating how context can fulfill the requirement of intention specification.

The Mukhtali'ah's Multiple Attributes: A Usul Dispute
Dispute on whether divorce applies to a woman who has initiated khul'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conflicting attributes of a mukhtali'ah in this usul dispute and state the Hanafi view that divorce applies due to her 'iddah, not her severed marriage.

Core Idea
A woman who initiates khul' is not a blank slate; her 'iddah preserves the valid marriage's effects.

The opponents argue that after khul', the marriage is severed, so no further divorce can apply. The Hanafis counter by shifting the relevant description: she is still in 'iddah from a valid marriage, and divorce applies because of that 'iddah. This teaches a crucial usul principle: a single entity can have multiple legal descriptions, each carrying its own ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Argument
The opponent says the mukhtali'ah is 'munqati'at an-nikah' (severed marriage) and analogizes her to a woman whose 'iddah has expired—both cannot receive a new divorce.
The opponent uses the attribute of severed marriage as the determining factor.
2
Recognize the Hanafi Response
The Hanafis accept the qiyas but redirect it: divorce does not apply due to 'severed marriage,' but it does apply due to another attribute: she is 'mu'taddah min nikah sahih' (in 'iddah from a valid marriage).
The Hanafis do not deny the severed marriage; they point to a coexisting attribute.
3
Extract the Usul Principle
The same person can bear multiple attributes; each attribute is independent for determining rules. The ruling follows the specific attribute relevant to the question.
This principle is applied in many fiqh rulings: e.g., a woman's custody rights versus marital rights.
4
Apply to the Case
For the question 'Does divorce apply?', the relevant attribute is her being in 'iddah (a period during which she is still attached to the previous marriage), not the fact that the marriage is over.
Thus, the Hanafi ruling is that divorce does apply to the mukhtali'ah during her 'iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك قولهم في المختلعة : إنها منقطعة النكاح، فلا يلحقها الطلاق، كمنقضية العدة. ونحن نقول بموجبه ؛ لأن الطلاق لا يلحقها بهذا الوصف، بل بوصف أنها معتدة عن نكاح صحيح.
And likewise their saying about the woman of khul': She has a severed marriage, so divorce does not apply to her, like a woman whose 'iddah has expired. And we say in accordance with its requirement: because divorce does not apply to her by this description, but by the description that she is in 'iddah from a valid marriage.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage addresses the status of a woman who has undergone khul' (divorce initiated by the wife) and is in her 'iddah (waiting period). The opponents argue that since her marriage is severed (munqati'ah an-nikah), divorce (from a subsequent marriage?) does not apply to her, analogous to a woman whose 'iddah has expired. The Hanafis agree with the requirement but specify that divorce does not apply under the description of severed marriage; however, under the description that she is in 'iddah from a valid marriage, divorce (from the same husband? Or a new divorce?) can apply. This highlights that the same woman can have multiple attributes each carrying different rulings.

Key Terms
مختلعة (mukhtali'ah): A woman who has initiated khul' (divorce for compensation).
منقطعة النكاح (munqati'at an-nikah): Severed marriage; indicating the marital bond is dissolved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because divorce is always possible

Key Takeaway
A mukhtali'ah is simultaneously a divorcée and a woman in 'iddah; the latter attribute justifies the application of divorce.

The Requirement of Faith in Emancipation for Expiation: Hanafi Response to Counterargument
Hanafi rebuttal to a qiyas-based objection regarding conditions for valid expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the requirement of faith in a slave for expiation and identify the legal reasoning (talil) used to defend the Lawgiver's authority over general rules.

Core Idea
The Lawgiver's authority can override the usual requirement of faith in expiatory emancipation, just as debt cancels guarantee.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection to the Hanafi view on expiation by emancipation. Opponents argue that freeing a slave for expiation is only valid if the slave is a believer, citing the analogy of expiation for killing. The Hanafis respond by affirming the general requirement but asserting the Lawgiver's absolute authority to set aside such conditions. This lesson unpacks the structure of the dispute and the underlying usul principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Claim
The opponent states: 'Emancipation in expiation does not effect expiation except with the faith of the emancipated, as in the expiation for killing.' Identify the qiyas (analogy) being used: expiation for killing requires a believing slave; therefore, any expiation via emancipation requires a believing slave.
The opponent is applying qiyas based on the illah of 'expiation'.
2
State the Hanafi Response
The Hanafis agree that the general description (wasf) of expiation necessitates faith. However, they argue that the existence of a mu'jib (cause or authority) can block (yamna') any opposition that would negate it. Here the mu'jib is the Lawgiver's absolute authority (itlaq sahib al-shar').
The Lawgiver is the 'owner of the right' (sahib al-haqq), analogous to a creditor.
3
Analyze the Analogy with Debt
The Hanafis illustrate: 'like debt that cancels the guarantee' (ka al-dayn yusqitu al-daman). Just as a debt obligation can nullify a guarantee (daman) when the debtor is the guarantor, the Lawgiver's command can nullify the usual condition of faith if He so wills.
This analogy highlights that the Lawgiver's authority is superior to any derived rule.
4
Extract the Usul Principle
The underlying principle is that qiyas can be overridden by a stronger dalil (here, the Lawgiver's explicit authority). The Hanafi school holds that the cause (sabab) for a ruling can be negated by the Lawgiver's direct intervention, similar to how a debt can preempt a guarantee.
This relates to the concept of 'mu'aradah' (contradiction) and tarjih (preference).
5
Evaluate the Practical Consequence
The practical result: While generally a slave must be a believer for valid expiation, if the Lawgiver explicitly allows emancipation of a non-believing slave (e.g., in a specific text or context), then that expiation is valid. The Hanafis do not deny the default rule but affirm the Lawgiver's power to make exceptions.
This shows that the Hanafi view is nuanced, not a blanket denial of the faith requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك قولهم: تحرير في تكفير، فلا يقع به التكفير إلا بإيمان المحرر، كما في كفارة القتل. ونحن نقول : هذا الوصف يوجب الإيمان عندنا، لكن قيام الموجب يمنع معارضة ما يُسقطه، وهو إطلاق صاحب الشرع، الذي هو صاحب الحق، كالدين يُسقط الضمان.
And from that is their saying: Emancipation in expiation, so expiation is not effected by it except by the faith of the emancipated person, as in the expiation of killing. And we say: This description necessitates faith according to us, but the existence of the cause prevents opposition that would negate it, which is the absolute authority of the Lawgiver, who is the owner of the right, like debt that cancels the guarantee.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage deals with expiation (kaffarah) through emancipating a slave. The opponents argue that such expiation is only valid if the emancipated slave is a believer (mu'min), analogous to the expiation for killing (which requires a believing slave). The Hanafis respond that while the description of expiation does require faith (i.e., the slave must be a believer according to the general rule), the Lawgiver's absolute authority can override this requirement. Just as a debt can cancel a guarantee (daman), the Lawgiver's direct command can negate the usual requirements. This implies that if the Lawgiver explicitly allows emancipation of a non-believing slave, it would be valid.

Key Terms
تكفير (takfir): Expiatory act to atone for a sin or violation.
إيمان المحرر (iman al-muharrar): The faith of the emancipated slave; being a believer.
الموجب (al-mujib): The cause or authority that necessitates a ruling; here, the Lawgiver's absolute authority.
ساقط الضمان (saqit al-daman): One who cancels the guarantee; refers to the creditor whose debt nullifies the guarantor's obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave must be male
B) The slave must be a believer
C) The slave must be adult
D) The slave must be healthy

Key Takeaway
Hanafis uphold the general requirement of faith in expiatory emancipation but affirm the Lawgiver's absolute authority to override it, as illustrated by the debt analogy.

Theft Description Does Not Prevent Cancellation by Absolution
Argument that description does not prevent cancellation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a legal description (wasf) does not immunize a ruling from cancellation, using the example of ibra' canceling the hadd for theft.

Core Idea
A legal description does not prevent cancellation by a later valid cause.

This short passage builds on a recurring theme in Hanafi usul: the relationship between a description (wasf) and its ruling. The opponents argue that defining theft as taking property without religious scruple necessarily entails the hadd punishment. The Hanafis agree with the definition but point out that it does not prevent other factors from canceling that ruling, such as the victim's absolution (ibra'). This principle is crucial for understanding how rulings can be modified by intervening causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the description
Locate the definition of theft given in the passage: taking another's property without religious scruple (la tadayyun). This is the wasf (description) that the opponents claim necessitates a specific ruling.
2
Note the intended ruling
The ruling that the description is said to necessitate is the hadd punishment (cutting of the hand). The text mentions 'execution of the hadd' at the end.
3
Recognize the canceling factor
The Hanafis argue that the description does not prevent the intervention of something that cancels the ruling. The example given is absolution (ibra') by the victim. Understand that ibra' can forgive the right to demand the hadd.
In Hanafi fiqh, ibra' can cancel both the property claim and the hadd under certain conditions.
4
Generalize the principle
The underlying principle is that a ruling based on a description is not fixed indefinitely; it can be overridden by another valid cause (sabab) or by the absence of a condition. This illustrates the Hanafi emphasis on the interplay of multiple legal factors.
This principle applies beyond theft: many rulings can be cancelled by forgiveness, waiver, or other causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك قولهم في السرقة : إنها أخذُ مال الغير بلا تدين فيوجـ قلنا: نحن نقول به، لكنه لا يمنع اعتراض ما يُسقطه، كالإبراء، فكذلك استيفاء الحد.
And likewise their saying about theft: It is taking the property of another without religious scruple [so it necessitates (had?)]. We said: We say that, but it does not prevent the intervention of something that cancels it, like absolution, and similarly the execution of the hadd punishment.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage discusses the description of theft (sariqah) as taking property without any religious justification (la tadayyun). The opponents likely argue that theft necessitates a specific ruling (such as cutting of the hand). The Hanafis agree with that description but point out that it does not prevent other factors from cancelling the ruling. For example, the victim's absolution (ibra') can cancel the obligation of returning the property or even the hadd punishment. Similarly, the execution of the hadd punishment itself can be cancelled by certain factors. This again illustrates that a ruling based on one description can be overridden by another valid cause.

Key Terms
سرقة (sariqah): Theft; taking another's property secretly and without right.
إبراء (ibra'): Legal absolution or release from a claim; forgiving a debt or right.
لا تدين (la tadayyun): Without religious scruple; taking property in a way that is not sanctioned by religion.

Key Takeaway

Principle that Description Does Not Guarantee Ruling
Series of Hanafi rebuttals to opponents' arguments about descriptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position that a legal description does not automatically determine a ruling, and apply this principle to distinguish between the description and overriding factors in four case examples from the text.

Core Idea
A description (wasf) alone does not guarantee a ruling; other factors can override.

This passage presents a distinctive Hanafi perspective on the relationship between descriptions (أوصاف) and legal rulings. While opponents argue that a particular description necessarily entails a specific ruling, the Hanafi school responds by accepting the relevance of the description but insisting that it does not prevent the ruling from being overridden by other considerations, such as invalid conditions, the Lawgiver's explicit release, or pardon. Examining four diverse examples — from salam contracts to theft — this lesson unpacks the nuanced Hanafi method of legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
For each example, determine what description the opponent uses to argue that a specific ruling must follow.
E.g., in the salam example, the opponent says because the commodity is measured (مذروع), the salam is permissible.
2
State the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school accept the description but deny its guarantee? The Hanafi may agree with the opponent's immediate ruling but on different grounds.
In the salam case, Hanafis say the description does not prevent invalidity if an invalid condition is attached.
3
Locate the overriding factor
What specific factor overrides the description in each case?
For salam: an invalid condition. For mukhalla'a: the description of being in 'idda from a valid marriage. For manumission: the Lawgiver's release. For theft: pardon.
4
Derive the underlying principle
Synthesize the general rule about descriptions and rulings from these examples.
The principle: 'The description alone is not a guarantee; other factors can nullify or alter the ruling.'
5
Apply to a new case
Given a new scenario, test whether a description alone would determine the ruling or if overriding factors exist.
E.g., a sale described as 'in good condition' — does that guarantee the sale is valid? No, because hidden defects may invalidate (option of defect).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير مضمونة بسائر الأوصاف.
ومن ذلك قولهم: أسلم مذروعاً في مذروع فجاز.
ونحن نقول: بهذا الوصف لا يفسد عندنا وذلك لا يمنع وجود الفساد بدليله كما إذا قرن به شرط فاسد.
وكذلك قولهم في المختلعة: إنها منقطعة النكاح، فلا يلحقها الطلاق، كمنقضية العدة.
ونحن نقول بموجبه؛ لأن الطلاق لا يلحقها بهذا الوصف، بل بوصف أنها معتدة عن نكاح صحيح.
ومن ذلك قولهم: تحرير في تكفير، فلا يقع به التكفير إلا بإيمان المحرر، كما في كفارة القتل.
ونحن نقول: هذا الوصف يوجب الإيمان عندنا، لكن قيام الموجب يمنع معارضة ما يُسقطه، وهو إطلاق صاحب الشرع، الذي هو صاحب الحق، كالدين يُسقط الضمان.
وكذلك قولهم في السرقة: إنها أخذ مال الغير بلا تدين فيوجب الحد.
قلنا: نحن نقول به، لكنه لا يمنع اعتراض ما يُسقطه، كالإبراء، فكذلك استيفاء الحد.
Not guaranteed by all descriptions. And from that is their saying: A salam contract for a measured thing in a measured thing is permissible. And we say: With this description it is not invalid according to us, and that does not prevent the existence of invalidity with its proof, just as if an invalid condition is attached to it. And likewise their saying regarding the mukhalla'a: She is one whose marriage is severed, so divorce does not affect her, like a woman whose 'idda has expired. And we say in response: Divorce does not affect her due to this description, but rather due to the description that she is in 'idda from a valid marriage. And from that is their saying: Manumission in expiation, so expiation does not take effect except with the faith of the manumitted, as in the expiation for killing. And we say: This description necessitates faith according to us, but the existence of the positive cause prevents opposition from what would nullify it, which is the release by the Lawgiver, who is the owner of the right, like debt that nullifies guarantee. And likewise their saying regarding theft: It is taking another's property without any religious sanction, so it necessitates the hadd punishment. We said: We affirm it, but it does not prevent the intervention of what would nullify it, like pardon, so likewise the execution of the hadd.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage is a series of Hanafi responses to opponents' arguments concerning the principle that a description (وصف) does not by itself guarantee a ruling. The text presents four examples where opponents claim that a specific description necessarily entails a legal ruling, while the Hanafi school argues that the description alone is insufficient and may be overridden by other factors. In the first example about salam (advance sale), opponents say salam in a measured commodity is permissible based on the description of being measured, but Hanafis respond that the description does not prevent invalidity if an invalid condition is attached, as the ruling depends on more than just the description. In the second example regarding a divorced woman (mukhalla'a) being in 'idda, opponents argue that since her marriage is severed, divorce does not affect her, similar to a woman whose 'idda has expired. Hanafis counter that divorce does not affect her due to the description of severed marriage, but due to the description of being in 'idda from a valid marriage. The third example concerns manumission in expiation: opponents claim that expiation does not take effect unless the manumitted person is a believer, but Hanafis hold that the description of manumission necessitates faith, but that positive cause can be opposed by the Lawgiver's explicit release, similar to a debt that nullifies a guarantee. The fourth example is about theft: opponents argue that taking property without religious sanction necessitates the hadd punishment, but Hanafis agree but note that the punishment can be nullified by other factors, like pardon. The underlying principle is that a description does not automatically determine a ruling; other conditions and preventers must be considered.

Key Terms
وصف (wasf): In this context, it means the attribute or characteristic cited as a cause for a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi responses in this passage, what is the main argument against the opponents' claims?
A) The description alone is sufficient for the ruling.
B) The description does not guarantee the ruling because other factors may override or nullify it.
C) The description is irrelevant and has no effect.
D) The description is valid only if accompanied by a condition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that a description does not guarantee a ruling requires considering all relevant factors, including overriding conditions and preventers.

The Thayyib Example: Consent and Consultation in Usul al-Fiqh
Incomplete example from a qiyas or istihsan discussion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of the thayyib example in Hanafi usul al-fiqh and infer the likely usul principle it illustrates.

Core Idea
The thayyib example demonstrates a ruling based on autonomy, not qiyas.

This fragment from a classical usul text presents a well-known fiqh ruling: a non-virgin (thayyib) whose opinion is sought cannot be forced into marriage. While the full argument is missing, this example likely serves as a counterexample in a discussion about qiyas or istihsan. By analyzing the fragment, we can explore how Hanafi jurists use such rulings to refine their usul principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the marriage of a thayyib (non-virgin). The ruling is that she cannot be married unwillingly (كرهاً). Determine the hukm level: it is haram to marry her without her consent.
This ruling is established in Hanafi fiqh, as the thayyib's consent is a condition.
2
Locate the basis of the ruling
The basis is implied by the phrase 'تُرجى مشورتها' (whose opinion is sought). This indicates that the woman's autonomy and consultation are paramount. The ruling is not derived from qiyas but from the general principle of respecting a woman's choice.
The basis might be a text (hadith) or consensus (ijma).
3
Determine the usul function of this example
In the usul text, this example is introduced with 'مثله' (similar to it), meaning it parallels a previous example. Likely, the author is using this ruling as a counterexample to challenge a qiyas argument: if someone claims a ruling by qiyas, this example shows that the ruling does not follow qiyas because it considers a specific attribute (being a thayyib whose opinion is sought).
This is a common technique in usul: objecting to qiyas by pointing out an illa that is not present.
4
Extrapolate the incomplete discussion
The full discussion likely involved an opponent (perhaps Shafi'i) who argued by qiyas that a thayyib could be married without consent, analogizing to a virgin. The Hanafi response would be that the thayyib's opinion is a distinguishing factor, breaking the analogy. The author then provides this example to illustrate.
The text cutting off prevents certainty, but this reconstruction is common in Hanafi usul.

Source Text

ومثله في قولهم : ثيب تُرجى مشورتها ، فلا تُنكح كَرهاً
And similar to it is their statement: a non-virgin whose opinion is sought, so she is not married unwillingly.

This fragment appears to be the start of another example of mumana'a, similar to the first lesson about a thayyib saghira. The text cuts off before any objection or response. It is included here as an incomplete unit; the full argument would mirror the earlier discussion about consent and opinion.

Qiyas and Istihsan: Rulings on Fainting and Insanity in Prayer
Comparing rulings on fainting and insanity in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the initial qiyas and the istihsan-based ruling for fainting and insanity regarding prayer obligations, and apply this methodology to other prolonged conditions.

Core Idea
Analogy says fainting doesn't drop prayers; istihsan says it does when prolonged. Hardship overrides strict analogy.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi presents a classic case of the Hanafi method. It illustrates how the school sometimes departs from qiyas using istihsan to avoid hardship. By examining fainting and insanity, we see that both conditions are treated similarly in fatwa, though their initial analogies differ. This lesson will guide you through the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify initial analogical rulings
The author states that by qiyas, fainting does not drop the prayer obligation because it is brief, while insanity drops it because it is prolonged. Note the starting point of each.
Qiyas based on duration is the default.
2
Recognize application of istihsan
The text says 'istahsanū fī al-kathīr' for fainting (they performed istihsan in extensive cases) and 'istahsanū fī al-qalīl' for insanity (they performed istihsan in brief cases). This means the final ruling overrides qiyas.
Istihsan departs from qiyas to prevent hardship.
3
Understand the 'illa (cause)
The common cause for overriding is prolonged duration leading to hardship (haraj). The text says 'li-annahumā sawā' fī al-ṭūl wa al-imtidād al-dā'ī ilā al-ḥaraj' – both are equal in length and extension that leads to hardship.
4
Compare with childhood and disbelief
Childhood (ṣabā) is also extended, so it is treated like insanity by analogy. Disbelief (kufr) is different because it negates legal capacity (ahliyyah) and the merit for afterlife reward.
The contrast helps delimit the ruling's scope.
5
Derive the final fatwa and methodological lesson
The fatwa for both fainting and insanity is the same: prolonged conditions drop the prayer obligation; brief ones do not. The methodological lesson is that istihsan overrides qiyas when strict analogy would cause hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا كلام في الحدود الفاصلة، ولا حرج في استغراق الإغماء؛ لأنه قلما يمتد شهرا، وفي الصلوات استوى الإغماء والجنون في الفتوى وإن اختلفا في الأصل، فكان القياس في الإغماء أن لا يُسقط. واستحسنوا في الكثير، وكان القياس في الجنون أن يُسقط، واستحسنوا في القليل؛ لأنهما سواء في الطول والامتداد الداعي إلى الحرج، والصبا ممتد أيضاً، فكان مثل الجنون. وبخلاف الكفر؛ لأنه ينافي الأهلية، وينافي استحقاق ثواب الآخرة، بخلاف الجنون.
There is no speech about the separating boundaries, and no hardship in the continuity of fainting because it rarely extends for a month. In prayers, fainting and insanity are equal in fatwa although they differ in origin. So the analogy for fainting was that it does not drop [the prayer], and they performed istihsan in much of it. And the analogy for insanity was that it drops [the prayer], and they performed istihsan in little of it, because they are both in length and extension which leads to hardship, and childhood also extends, so it is like insanity. And unlike disbelief, because it contradicts legal capacity and contradicts the deserving of reward in the afterlife, unlike insanity.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul principle of qiyas and istihsan applied to the rulings of fainting (ighma) and insanity (junun) regarding the obligation of prayers. The author states that both are equal in the final ruling (fatwa) for prayers, though they differ in their original legal basis. By qiyas, fainting should not nullify the obligation because it is brief, but istihsan led to nullification in extensive cases. Conversely, qiyas would nullify in insanity, but istihsan applies in brief cases. This is because both conditions, when prolonged, cause hardship. Childhood (saba) is also prolonged, thus treated like insanity. Disbelief (kufr) is distinguished because it destroys legal capacity and merit for the afterlife, unlike insanity.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning to derive a ruling from an original case based on shared effective cause ('illa).
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of overriding the analogical ruling in favor of a more preferable ruling based on evidence or facilitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, why is childhood (ṣabā) treated like insanity (junūn) rather than like fainting (ighmā) in the ruling on prayer obligations?
A) Because childhood is a prolonged state leading to hardship.
B) Because children are not legally obligated.
C) Because childhood involves loss of mind like insanity.
D) Because of a direct Quranic text.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, istihsan can override qiyas when strict analogy leads to hardship, as seen in the rulings for prolonged fainting and insanity.
